Rulings of the Haram: The Sanctity of Mecca and Its Legal Consequences
Lists special Haram rulings as furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'لا يدخله أحد إلا محرمًا' to derive the special rulings of the Haram and explain their fiqh consequences.

Core Idea
The Haram of Mecca has a unique sanctity that modifies normal rulings: entry requires ihram, plants and game are protected, and residence is prohibited for non-Muslims.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair enumerates the special legal provisions that apply exclusively to the Sacred Precinct of Mecca. The rulings are derived from the overarching principle that the Haram is a protected sanctuary with heightened spiritual gravity. The passage functions as a concise list of exceptional rules, providing a foundation for the mufti to apply them in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the governing qawida
Identify the maxim: 'لا يدخله أحد إلا محرمًا' (No one enters it except in ihram). This is the overarching principle from which other rulings branch.
This is a dabt (sub-maxim) that restricts the general rule of entry.
2
Enumerate the furuat (branch rulings)
List each specific ruling mentioned: entry requires ihram, residence is makruh, no killing/amputation for refugees, game is sacred, trees/grass protected except idhkhir, ghusl recommended, prayers multiplied, deeds magnified, intention accountable, non-Muslim entry allowed but residence prohibited, no tamattu'/qiran from within.
Each furu' is a legal consequence of the Haram's sanctity.
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The common factor is the sanctity (hurma) of the Haram itself. This illa triggers heightened prohibitions and obligations.
The illa is not stated explicitly but derived from the context.
4
Note the istithna (exception)
The only exception is idhkhir grass, which may be cut and grazed based on a hadith. This reinforces the generality of the prohibition.
Istithna confirms the rule: all other plants are protected.
5
Synthesize the mufta bihi positions
All rulings listed are standard Hanafi positions (mufta bihi). The mufti applies them directly, with awareness of the exception and the scope of each prohibition.
These are from later Hanafi texts (Ashbah) but reflect established doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في أحكام الحرم لا يدخله أحد إلا محرمًا وتكره المجاورة به، ولا يقتل ولا يقطع من فعل خارجه والتجأ به ويحرم التعرّض لصيده ويجب الجزاء بقتله، ويحرم قطع شجره ورعي حشيشه إلا الإذخر ويسنّ الغسل لدخوله وتضاعف فيه الصلاة، وحسناته كسيئاته، ويؤاخذ فيه بالهم ولا يسكن فيه كافر، وله الدخول فيه، ولا تمتع ولا قران .
Section on the rulings of the Haram [Sacred Precinct]: No one enters it except in a state of ihram, and residing in it is disliked. No one is killed or amputated [in terms of penalties] for an act committed outside it and then takes refuge in it. It is prohibited to disturb its game, and compensation is obligatory for killing it. It is prohibited to cut its trees or graze its grass except al-idhkhir. Ghusl is recommended upon entering it. Prayer is multiplied in it. Its good deeds and evil deeds [are multiplied] alike. One is held accountable even for an intention. A disbeliever may not reside in it, but he may enter it. Neither tamattu' nor qiran [are allowed in the Haram? The text seems abbreviated; possibly meaning these forms of Hajj are not permitted in the Haram itself?]
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the Haram (the Sacred Precinct of Mecca) sets forth the special legal rulings that apply due to its sanctity. The Haram is uniquely honored: no one may enter it unless in the state of ihram. Prolonged residence (mujawara) there is disliked (makruh). The Haram also affects criminal law: a person who committed a crime outside the Haram and seeks refuge inside may not be killed or amputated (i.e., criminal penalties are suspended while within the sanctuary). Hunting within the Haram is prohibited, and if one kills game, compensation (jaza') is due. Cutting trees or grazing plants is forbidden, with the exception of idhkhir grass. It is recommended (sunna) to perform ghusl before entering. The reward for prayers is multiplied, and similarly, both good and evil deeds are magnified—one is even held accountable for mere intentions (al-hamm). A disbeliever may not reside permanently in the Haram, but may enter temporarily. The phrase 'ولا تمتع ولا قران' likely refers to the prohibition of performing Tamattu' and Qiran Hajj from within the Haram, but the text is abbreviated; it may mean that one cannot combine Umra and Hajj in Tamattu' or Qiran while in the Haram itself? Actually it is ambiguous. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
حرم (haram): The sacred precinct of Mecca, with specific legal sanctity.
إذخر (idhkhir): A type of sweet-smelling grass that is exempt from the prohibition of cutting plants in the Haram.
مجاورة (mujawara): Residing or staying long-term in the Haram.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation for killing game in the Haram, such as sacrificing an equivalent animal or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding cutting trees in the Haram?
A) It is completely permissible
B) It is prohibited except for the idhkhir grass
C) It is only prohibited during Hajj
D) It is permitted for residents only

Key Takeaway
The Haram's unique sanctity imposes heightened obligations: entry in ihram, protection of wildlife and vegetation, and prohibition of non-Muslim residence, with only idhkhir grass exempted from the plant protection.

The Prohibition of Entering a Dead Body into the Mosque
Dispute analysis on cause of dead body prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bringing a dead body into the mosque, explain the ikhtilaf regarding its cause, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The prohibition of bringing a dead body into the mosque is tahrim (sinful), not due to impurity but to avoid soiling the sanctified space.

Among the many rulings on mosque sanctity, the case of the dead body is particularly nuanced. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the prohibition is strict (tahrim) or merely disliked (tanzih), and the preferred view hinges on the cause (illa). This lesson dissects that reasoning and shows how the tarjih is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states it is prohibited to bring a dead body into the mosque. Note that praying the funeral prayer inside is also prevented unless there is an excuse (like rain).
The ruling covers both the body and the prayer.
2
Locate the ikhtilaf
Two opinions exist: (1) prohibition due to fear of soiling, (2) prohibition because the mosque was not built for funerals. The first yields tahrim, the second tanzih.
The cause (illa) determines the legal weight.
3
Analyze the tarjih
Al-Allamah Qasim preferred the first view (tahrim). The text also notes that no one attributed the prohibition to the impurity of the dead body, as it is pure after washing by consensus.
The tarjih is based on avoiding all possibility of soiling, which aligns with the maxim of blocking means (sadd al-dhara'i').
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is that entering a dead body is haram and sinful. An exception is made for valid excuses like rain.
A mufti must follow this view, as it is the relied-upon position.
5
Distinguish from secondary issues
The permissibility of i'tikaf and other acts remain valid. This ruling is specific to the dead body, not to other impure items.
The principle is about protecting the mosque's sanctity, not a general ban on entry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمكّي وتختص الهدايا به ويُكره إخراج حجارته وترابه وهو مُساوِ لغيره عندنا في اللقطة، والدّية على القاتل فيه خطأ، ولا حرم للمدينة عندنا فلا تثبت هذه الأحكام إلا استنان الغسل لدخولها وكراهة المجاورة بها والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم . حدة . هي القول في أحكام المسجد كثيرة جدا وقد ذكرها أصحاب الفتاوى في كتاب الصلاة في باب على فمنها تحريم دخوله على الجُنُب والحائض والنفساء ولو على وجه العبور، وإدخال نجاسة فيه يُخاف منها التلويث ومنع إدخال الميت فيه؛ والصحيح أن المنع الصلاة الجنازة وإن لم يكن الميت فيه إلا لعذر مطر ونحوه واختلفوا في علته، فمنهم من علَّل بخوف التلويث، ومنهم من علله بأنه لم يبن لها. وعلى الأول هي تحريمية، وعلى الثاني هي تنزيهية، ورجح الأول العلامة قاسم رحمه الله تعالى. ولم يعلله أحد منا بنجاسة الميت لإجماعهم على طهارته بالغسل إن كان مسلما. ومنها : صحة الاعتكاف فيه . ومنها حرمة إدخال الصبيان والمجانين حيث غلب تنجيسهم وإلا فيُكرَه . ومنها : منع القاء القملة بعد قتلها فيه . ومنها: تحريم البول فيه ولو في إناء، وأما الفصد فيه في إناء فلم أره؛ وينبغي أن لا فرق . ومنها : منع أخذ شيء من أجزائه. قالوا في ترابه إن كان مجتمعا جاز الأخذ منه ومسح الرجل عليه وإلا لا . ومنها : حُرمة البصاق فيه وإلقاء النخامة فوق الحصير أخف من وضعها تحته، فإن اضطر إليه دفنه. وتُكرَه المضمضة والوضوء فيه إلا أن يكون ثمة موضع أُعِدَّ لذلك لا يُصلَّى فيه، أو في إناء. ويُكرّه مسح الرجل من الطين على عموده والبزاق على حيطانه. ولا يُحفّر فيه بئر ماء وتُترك القديمة، ويُكرَه غسل الأشجار فيه إلا لمنفعة ليقل النزّ، ولا يجوز اتخاذ طريق فيه للمرور إلا لعذر ، وتكره الصناعة فيه من خياطة وكتابة بأجر وتعليم صبيان بأجر لا بغيره إلا لحفظ المسجد في رواية، ويُكرَه الجلوس فيه للمصيبة، وتُستَحَب التحية لداخله. فإن كان ممن يتكرر دخوله كفته ركعتان كل يوم، ويُستحب عقد النكاح فيه وجلوس القاضي فيه، ويحرم الوطء فيه منه، وكذا كل مؤذ فيه كريهة ويمنع منه، ريح وفوقه كالتخلّي، ويُكره دخوله لمن أكل ذا ولو بلسانه، ومن البيع والشراء وكل عقد لغير المعتكف ويجوز له بقدر حاجته إن لم يحضر السلعة، وإنشاد الضالة والأشعار والأكل والنوم لغير غريب ومعتكف والكلام المباح وفي فتح القدير أنه يأكل الحسنات كما تأكل النار الحطب ورفع الصوت بالذكر إلا للمتفقهة وإخراج الريح فيه من الدبر والخصومة، ويسنّ كنسه وتنظيفه وفرشه ،وإيقاده وتقديم اليمنى على اليسرى عند دخوله وعكسه عند خروجه، ومن اعتاد المرور فيه يأثم ويفسق ويُكرَه تخصيص مكان فيه لصلاته، ولا يتعين بالملازمة فلا يزعج غيره لو سبقه إليه ولأهل المحلة جعل المسجد الواحد مسجدين والأولى أن يكون لكل طائفة مؤذن، ولهم جعل المسجدين واحدا، ولا تجوز إعارة أدواته لمسجد آخر، ولا يشغل المسجد بالمتاع إلا للخوف في الفتنة العامة . خاتمة : أعظم المساجد حرمة المسجد الحرام ثم مسجد المدينة ثم مسجد بيت المقدس ثم الجوامع ثم مساجد المحال ثم مساجد الشوارع ثم مساجد البيوت .
For Makkah, gifts are specified to it, and it is disliked to take out its stones and dust. It is equal to other places in our view regarding lost property. The blood-money for killing there by mistake is (due). There is no sanctity for Madinah in our view, so these rulings are not established except the recommendation of ghusl for entering it and dislike of residing there. Allah knows best. Chapter. This is the chapter on rulings of the mosque. They are many, and the authors of fatwas have mentioned them in the Book of Prayer in the chapter on... Among them: prohibition of entering it for the junub, menstruating woman, and postpartum woman, even if just passing through. And entering impurity that is feared to soil it, and prevention of bringing a dead body into it. The correct view is that praying the funeral prayer is prevented even if the dead body is not inside except for an excuse like rain and the like. They differed regarding its cause. Some said it is due to fear of soiling, others said because the mosque was not built for it. On the first, the prohibition is for tahrim, on the second it is for tanzih. Al-Allamah Qasim (may Allah have mercy on him) preferred the first. None of us attributed it to the impurity of the dead body due to consensus that it becomes pure by washing if Muslim. Among them: validity of i'tikaf in it. Among them: prohibition of bringing in boys and insane persons where their soiling is predominant, otherwise it is disliked. Among them: prevention of throwing a louse after killing it in it. Among them: prohibition of urinating in it even in a vessel. As for phlebotomy in a vessel, I have not seen it; it should be that there is no difference. Among them: prevention of taking any part of its components. They said regarding its dust: if it is collected, it is permissible to take some and wipe the feet with it, otherwise not. Among them: prohibition of spitting in it, and expelling phlegm above the mat is lighter than placing it under it. If forced, he buries it. Rinsing the mouth and performing wudu in it are disliked unless there is a place prepared for that where prayer is not performed, or in a vessel. Wiping the foot with clay on its pillar and spitting on its walls are disliked. A well for water should not be dug in it, and old ones are left. Washing trees in it is disliked except for benefit to reduce moisture. It is not permissible to make it a thoroughfare for passage except for an excuse. Craftsmanship in it for sewing and writing for payment is disliked, and teaching boys for payment (is disliked) but not without it except for preserving the mosque in one narration. Sitting in it for a calamity is disliked. Greeting for its enterer is recommended. If one enters repeatedly, two rak'ahs each day suffice him. Holding marriage contracts in it and the judge sitting in it are recommended. Sexual intercourse in it is forbidden. Also, any harmful thing in it is disliked and prevented, (such as) wind (breaking wind) and above it like relieving oneself. It is disliked for one who has eaten garlic to enter it, even if (only) with his tongue. (Among them) buying and selling and any contract for other than the mu'takif (are disliked), and it is permissible for him to the extent of his need if he does not present the commodity. (Among them) announcing a lost item and poems, eating and sleeping for other than a stranger and mu'takif, and permissible speech (are disliked). In Fath al-Qadir, it (permissible speech) eats up good deeds as fire eats up firewood. Raising the voice in dhikr except for students of fiqh, and passing wind and disputes (are disliked). Sweeping, cleaning, and furnishing it are sunnah, as is lighting it. Entering with the right foot first and exiting with the left foot (are sunnah). One accustomed to passing through it sins and becomes a transgressor. It is disliked to designate a specific spot in it for one's prayer. It is not determined by continuous use, so he should not disturb another if someone preceded him. The people of the neighborhood may make one mosque into two, and it is better that each group has its own mu'adhdhin. They may also make two mosques into one. Lending its tools to another mosque is not permissible. The mosque should not be occupied with goods except for fear in a general fitna. Conclusion: The greatest mosque in sanctity is al-Masjid al-Haram, then the Prophet's Mosque, then Masjid Bayt al-Maqdis, then the congregational mosques, then the mosques of neighborhoods, then street mosques, then house mosques.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings (furu') regarding mosques, presented as a list from fatwa collections. It begins with a brief note on the specific rulings of Makkah (gifts, removing stones/dust, equal in lost property, blood-money for accidental killing) and Madinah (no sanctity except ghusl and dislike of residence). Then it transitions to the main section on mosque rulings. The first ruling is the prohibition of entry for the junub, menstruating, and postpartum women, even by passing through. Then it addresses the prohibition of bringing impurity that may soil the mosque, and the prevention of bringing a dead body. Importantly, there is an ikhtilaf (dispute) on the cause of this prohibition: some say it is to avoid soiling (tahrim), others say it is because the mosque was not built for funeral prayers (tanzih). The relied-upon view (tarjih) of al-Allamah Qasim is that it is tahrim (prohibition of closure). The text then lists many more rulings: validity of i'tikaf, prohibition of bringing boys/mad persons if they often soil, killing lice, urination (even in vessel), taking parts of the mosque (e.g., dust), spitting/flinging phlegm, gargling/wudu, wiping feet, digging wells, washing trees, making it a thoroughfare, crafts for payment, sitting for condolences, greeting upon entry, two rakahs for regular visitors, marriage contracts and judge sitting, prohibition of intercourse and harmful actions, prohibition for garlic-eaters, buying/selling except for mu'takif, announcing lost items, poems, eating/sleeping for non-residents and mu'takif, permissible speech (which eats good deeds), raising voice in dhikr (except for students), passing wind, disputes, sunnah of cleaning and entering/exiting etiquette, prohibition of passing through habitually, dislike of designating a spot, converting mosque configurations, and lending tools. The conclusion ranks mosques in sanctity: Makkah, Madinah, Jerusalem, jami', neighborhood, street, house. All these rulings are based on the principle that the mosque is a place of worship and must be respected and kept clean.

Key Terms
الميت (al-mayyit): A deceased person whose body is being considered for entering the mosque.
تحريمية (tahrimiyyah): Strict prohibition, making the act sinful.
تنزيهية (tanzihiyyah): Disliked but not sinful.
علة (illah): Effective cause behind a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (preferred view) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on entering a dead body into the mosque?
A) It is permissible if the body is washed
B) It is haram (tahrim) even if washed
C) It is makruh (tanzih)
D) It depends on the gender of the deceased

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits entering a dead body into the mosque as a strict prohibition (tahrim) to preserve sanctity, with an exception for genuine excuses like rain.

General Rulings of Mosque Sanctity: Cleanliness and Conduct
Compilation of furu' rulings on mosque conduct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the major rulings governing cleanliness, conduct, and prohibited acts in the mosque based on the principle of sanctity.

Core Idea
The mosque is a sacred space; all actions that compromise its purity, disrupt worship, or introduce worldly activities are restricted.

This lesson groups the many specific rulings from the fatwa collections that protect the mosque's sanctity. From prohibiting urination to forbidding buying and selling, each ruling is a furu' of the overarching maxim: preserve the mosque as a pure place of worship. We will explore the logic behind each restriction and how they apply in modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching principle
All the rulings derive from the sanctity (hurma) of the mosque. Acts that are normally permissible elsewhere become restricted inside the mosque.
This is a type of 'ta'zim' (veneration) that imposes additional rules.
2
Categorize the rulings
Group them into: (A) rulings on ritual purity (entering while impure, urination, etc.), (B) rulings on physical cleanliness (spitting, phlegm, dust), (C) rulings on worldly activities (buying, selling, crafts), (D) rulings on etiquette (voice, eating, sleeping), (E) others.
Use the text to list examples under each category.
3
Analyze the gradation of prohibition
Some acts are haram (e.g., urination), some are makruh tahrimi (e.g., selling), some are makruh tanzihi (e.g., eating). The severity depends on how much they violate sanctity.
Note the distinctions: najasa vs. mere disrespect.
4
Apply the rulings to modern scenarios
Consider: using smartphones for business, bringing food, cleaning methods. The rulings can be extended via qiyas.
Use the principle to deduce new rulings not explicitly mentioned.
5
Recognize exceptions
For urgent needs (e.g., passing through, teaching for preservation), some prohibitions are lifted. The mufti must balance sanctity and necessity.
The text mentions exceptions like 'for preservation of the mosque'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمكّي وتختص الهدايا به ويُكره إخراج حجارته وترابه وهو مُساوِ لغيره عندنا في اللقطة، والدّية على القاتل فيه خطأ، ولا حرم للمدينة عندنا فلا تثبت هذه الأحكام إلا استنان الغسل لدخولها وكراهة المجاورة بها والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم . حدة . هي القول في أحكام المسجد كثيرة جدا وقد ذكرها أصحاب الفتاوى في كتاب الصلاة في باب على فمنها تحريم دخوله على الجُنُب والحائض والنفساء ولو على وجه العبور، وإدخال نجاسة فيه يُخاف منها التلويث ومنع إدخال الميت فيه؛ والصحيح أن المنع الصلاة الجنازة وإن لم يكن الميت فيه إلا لعذر مطر ونحوه واختلفوا في علته، فمنهم من علَّل بخوف التلويث، ومنهم من علله بأنه لم يبن لها. وعلى الأول هي تحريمية، وعلى الثاني هي تنزيهية، ورجح الأول العلامة قاسم رحمه الله تعالى. ولم يعلله أحد منا بنجاسة الميت لإجماعهم على طهارته بالغسل إن كان مسلما. ومنها : صحة الاعتكاف فيه . ومنها حرمة إدخال الصبيان والمجانين حيث غلب تنجيسهم وإلا فيُكرَه . ومنها : منع القاء القملة بعد قتلها فيه . ومنها: تحريم البول فيه ولو في إناء، وأما الفصد فيه في إناء فلم أره؛ وينبغي أن لا فرق . ومنها : منع أخذ شيء من أجزائه. قالوا في ترابه إن كان مجتمعا جاز الأخذ منه ومسح الرجل عليه وإلا لا . ومنها : حُرمة البصاق فيه وإلقاء النخامة فوق الحصير أخف من وضعها تحته، فإن اضطر إليه دفنه. وتُكرَه المضمضة والوضوء فيه إلا أن يكون ثمة موضع أُعِدَّ لذلك لا يُصلَّى فيه، أو في إناء. ويُكرّه مسح الرجل من الطين على عموده والبزاق على حيطانه. ولا يُحفّر فيه بئر ماء وتُترك القديمة، ويُكرَه غسل الأشجار فيه إلا لمنفعة ليقل النزّ، ولا يجوز اتخاذ طريق فيه للمرور إلا لعذر ، وتكره الصناعة فيه من خياطة وكتابة بأجر وتعليم صبيان بأجر لا بغيره إلا لحفظ المسجد في رواية، ويُكرَه الجلوس فيه للمصيبة، وتُستَحَب التحية لداخله. فإن كان ممن يتكرر دخوله كفته ركعتان كل يوم، ويُستحب عقد النكاح فيه وجلوس القاضي فيه، ويحرم الوطء فيه منه، وكذا كل مؤذ فيه كريهة ويمنع منه، ريح وفوقه كالتخلّي، ويُكره دخوله لمن أكل ذا ولو بلسانه، ومن البيع والشراء وكل عقد لغير المعتكف ويجوز له بقدر حاجته إن لم يحضر السلعة، وإنشاد الضالة والأشعار والأكل والنوم لغير غريب ومعتكف والكلام المباح وفي فتح القدير أنه يأكل الحسنات كما تأكل النار الحطب ورفع الصوت بالذكر إلا للمتفقهة وإخراج الريح فيه من الدبر والخصومة، ويسنّ كنسه وتنظيفه وفرشه ،وإيقاده وتقديم اليمنى على اليسرى عند دخوله وعكسه عند خروجه، ومن اعتاد المرور فيه يأثم ويفسق ويُكرَه تخصيص مكان فيه لصلاته، ولا يتعين بالملازمة فلا يزعج غيره لو سبقه إليه ولأهل المحلة جعل المسجد الواحد مسجدين والأولى أن يكون لكل طائفة مؤذن، ولهم جعل المسجدين واحدا، ولا تجوز إعارة أدواته لمسجد آخر، ولا يشغل المسجد بالمتاع إلا للخوف في الفتنة العامة . خاتمة : أعظم المساجد حرمة المسجد الحرام ثم مسجد المدينة ثم مسجد بيت المقدس ثم الجوامع ثم مساجد المحال ثم مساجد الشوارع ثم مساجد البيوت .
For Makkah, gifts are specified to it, and it is disliked to take out its stones and dust. It is equal to other places in our view regarding lost property. The blood-money for killing there by mistake is (due). There is no sanctity for Madinah in our view, so these rulings are not established except the recommendation of ghusl for entering it and dislike of residing there. Allah knows best. Chapter. This is the chapter on rulings of the mosque. They are many, and the authors of fatwas have mentioned them in the Book of Prayer in the chapter on... Among them: prohibition of entering it for the junub, menstruating woman, and postpartum woman, even if just passing through. And entering impurity that is feared to soil it, and prevention of bringing a dead body into it. The correct view is that praying the funeral prayer is prevented even if the dead body is not inside except for an excuse like rain and the like. They differed regarding its cause. Some said it is due to fear of soiling, others said because the mosque was not built for it. On the first, the prohibition is for tahrim, on the second it is for tanzih. Al-Allamah Qasim (may Allah have mercy on him) preferred the first. None of us attributed it to the impurity of the dead body due to consensus that it becomes pure by washing if Muslim. Among them: validity of i'tikaf in it. Among them: prohibition of bringing in boys and insane persons where their soiling is predominant, otherwise it is disliked. Among them: prevention of throwing a louse after killing it in it. Among them: prohibition of urinating in it even in a vessel. As for phlebotomy in a vessel, I have not seen it; it should be that there is no difference. Among them: prevention of taking any part of its components. They said regarding its dust: if it is collected, it is permissible to take some and wipe the feet with it, otherwise not. Among them: prohibition of spitting in it, and expelling phlegm above the mat is lighter than placing it under it. If forced, he buries it. Rinsing the mouth and performing wudu in it are disliked unless there is a place prepared for that where prayer is not performed, or in a vessel. Wiping the foot with clay on its pillar and spitting on its walls are disliked. A well for water should not be dug in it, and old ones are left. Washing trees in it is disliked except for benefit to reduce moisture. It is not permissible to make it a thoroughfare for passage except for an excuse. Craftsmanship in it for sewing and writing for payment is disliked, and teaching boys for payment (is disliked) but not without it except for preserving the mosque in one narration. Sitting in it for a calamity is disliked. Greeting for its enterer is recommended. If one enters repeatedly, two rak'ahs each day suffice him. Holding marriage contracts in it and the judge sitting in it are recommended. Sexual intercourse in it is forbidden. Also, any harmful thing in it is disliked and prevented, (such as) wind (breaking wind) and above it like relieving oneself. It is disliked for one who has eaten garlic to enter it, even if (only) with his tongue. (Among them) buying and selling and any contract for other than the mu'takif (are disliked), and it is permissible for him to the extent of his need if he does not present the commodity. (Among them) announcing a lost item and poems, eating and sleeping for other than a stranger and mu'takif, and permissible speech (are disliked). In Fath al-Qadir, it (permissible speech) eats up good deeds as fire eats up firewood. Raising the voice in dhikr except for students of fiqh, and passing wind and disputes (are disliked). Sweeping, cleaning, and furnishing it are sunnah, as is lighting it. Entering with the right foot first and exiting with the left foot (are sunnah). One accustomed to passing through it sins and becomes a transgressor. It is disliked to designate a specific spot in it for one's prayer. It is not determined by continuous use, so he should not disturb another if someone preceded him. The people of the neighborhood may make one mosque into two, and it is better that each group has its own mu'adhdhin. They may also make two mosques into one. Lending its tools to another mosque is not permissible. The mosque should not be occupied with goods except for fear in a general fitna. Conclusion: The greatest mosque in sanctity is al-Masjid al-Haram, then the Prophet's Mosque, then Masjid Bayt al-Maqdis, then the congregational mosques, then the mosques of neighborhoods, then street mosques, then house mosques.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings (furu') regarding mosques, presented as a list from fatwa collections. It begins with a brief note on the specific rulings of Makkah (gifts, removing stones/dust, equal in lost property, blood-money for accidental killing) and Madinah (no sanctity except ghusl and dislike of residence). Then it transitions to the main section on mosque rulings. The first ruling is the prohibition of entry for the junub, menstruating, and postpartum women, even by passing through. Then it addresses the prohibition of bringing impurity that may soil the mosque, and the prevention of bringing a dead body. Importantly, there is an ikhtilaf (dispute) on the cause of this prohibition: some say it is to avoid soiling (tahrim), others say it is because the mosque was not built for funeral prayers (tanzih). The relied-upon view (tarjih) of al-Allamah Qasim is that it is tahrim (prohibition of closure). The text then lists many more rulings: validity of i'tikaf, prohibition of bringing boys/mad persons if they often soil, killing lice, urination (even in vessel), taking parts of the mosque (e.g., dust), spitting/flinging phlegm, gargling/wudu, wiping feet, digging wells, washing trees, making it a thoroughfare, crafts for payment, sitting for condolences, greeting upon entry, two rakahs for regular visitors, marriage contracts and judge sitting, prohibition of intercourse and harmful actions, prohibition for garlic-eaters, buying/selling except for mu'takif, announcing lost items, poems, eating/sleeping for non-residents and mu'takif, permissible speech (which eats good deeds), raising voice in dhikr (except for students), passing wind, disputes, sunnah of cleaning and entering/exiting etiquette, prohibition of passing through habitually, dislike of designating a spot, converting mosque configurations, and lending tools. The conclusion ranks mosques in sanctity: Makkah, Madinah, Jerusalem, jami', neighborhood, street, house. All these rulings are based on the principle that the mosque is a place of worship and must be respected and kept clean.

Key Terms
الجنب (al-junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
النخامة (al-nukhamah): Phlegm from the chest or throat.
المعتكف (al-mu'takif): A person observing i'tikaf (seclusion in mosque for worship).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is makruh (disliked) but not haram in the mosque according to the text?
A) Spitting on the floor
B) Urinating in a vessel
C) Eating for a non-gharib (non-stranger)
D) Announcing a lost object

Key Takeaway
The sanctity of the mosque imposes a broad set of restrictions; muftis must apply the general principle of preservation of purity and worship to new situations.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
beginner
6 min
The Hierarchy of Mosque Sanctity in Hanafi Fiqh
Conclusion ranking mosque sanctity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven-tier hierarchy of mosque sanctity according to the Hanafi school and explain the basis for the ranking.

Core Idea
Not all mosques have equal sanctity; the most sacred are al-Masjid al-Haram, then the Prophet's Mosque, then al-Aqsa, then congregational mosques, then neighborhood, street, and house mosques.

The passage concludes with a ranking of mosques by their degree of sanctity. This hierarchy has practical implications for rulings such as the obligation of prayer in congregation, the reward of prayer, and certain prohibitions. Understanding this hierarchy helps muftis prioritize violations when multiple mosques are involved.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the order
The text lists seven levels: 1) Masjid al-Haram, 2) Masjid al-Nabawi, 3) Masjid Bayt al-Maqdis (al-Aqsa), 4) al-Jawami' (congregational mosques), 5) Masajid al-Mahall (neighborhood mosques), 6) Masajid al-Shawari' (street mosques), 7) Masajid al-Buyut (house mosques).
This is a mnemonic for fatwa purposes.
2
Identify the basis of ranking
The ranking is based on the virtue and historical significance of the location, as well as the number of worshippers and the status of the mosque. Masjid al-Haram is the most sacred because of the Ka'bah, etc.
Note that house mosques (private prayer rooms) are at the lowest tier.
3
Apply the hierarchy
The hierarchy affects rulings: e.g., the reward for prayer multiplies more in higher-ranked mosques. Certain prohibitions (like bringing a dead body) are stricter in higher-ranked mosques.
The mufti may need to consider which mosque is affected when issuing a ruling.
4
Distinguish from other schools
This ranking is specific to the Hanafi school; others may have different orders (e.g., some consider Madinah equal to Makkah in certain aspects).
But within Hanafi, this is the relied-upon view.
5
Recall exceptions
The text earlier noted that Madinah has 'no sanctity' in terms of certain rulings (blood-money etc.), but here it is ranked second. This means sanctity here refers to spiritual virtue, not legal rules.
The mufti must be careful not to conflate different types of sanctity.

Source Text

Commentary

It begins with a brief note on the specific rulings of Makkah (gifts, removing stones/dust, equal in lost property, blood-money for accidental killing) and Madinah (no sanctity except ghusl and dislike of residence). Then it transitions to the main section on mosque rulings. The first ruling is the prohibition of entry for the junub, menstruating, and postpartum women, even by passing through. Then it addresses the prohibition of bringing impurity that may soil the mosque, and the prevention of bringing a dead body. Importantly, there is an ikhtilaf (dispute) on the cause of this prohibition: some say it is to avoid soiling (tahrim), others say it is because the mosque was not built for funeral prayers (tanzih). The relied-upon view (tarjih) of al-Allamah Qasim is that it is tahrim (prohibition of closure). The text then lists many more rulings: validity of i'tikaf, prohibition of bringing boys/mad persons if they often soil, killing lice, urination (even in vessel), taking parts of the mosque (e.g., dust), spitting/flinging phlegm, gargling/wudu, wiping feet, digging wells, washing trees, making it a thoroughfare, crafts for payment, sitting for condolences, greeting upon entry, two rakahs for regular visitors, marriage contracts and judge sitting, prohibition of intercourse and harmful actions, prohibition for garlic-eaters, buying/selling except for mu'takif, announcing lost items, poems, eating/sleeping for non-residents and mu'takif, permissible speech (which eats good deeds), raising voice in dhikr (except for students), passing wind, disputes, sunnah of cleaning and entering/exiting etiquette, prohibition of passing through habitually, dislike of designating a spot, converting mosque configurations, and lending tools. The conclusion ranks mosques in sanctity: Makkah, Madinah, Jerusalem, jami', neighborhood, street, house. All these rulings are based on the principle that the mosque is a place of worship and must be respected and kept clean.

Key Terms
الجوامع (al-jawami'): Congregational mosques where Friday prayer is held.
مساجد المحال (masajid al-mahall): Local neighborhood mosques.
مساجد البيوت (masajid al-buyut): Private prayer rooms in houses designated as masjids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ranking, which of the following is considered the most sacred mosque?
A) Masjid al-Nabawi
B) Masjid al-Haram
C) Masjid Bayt al-Maqdis
D) Al-Jami' (congregational mosque)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides a clear hierarchy of mosque sanctity that muftis use to differentiate rulings and rewards.

Rulings and Virtues of Friday (Yawm al-Jumu'ah)
Lists special rulings and virtues of Friday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific rulings and virtues of Friday according to the Hanafi school and apply them to practical iftaa scenarios.

Core Idea
Friday is a day of unique obligations, recommended acts, and profound virtues – a day of worship, mercy, and divine visitation.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir serves as a comprehensive reference for the mufti regarding Friday. It outlines the obligatory, recommended, and disliked actions, as well as the spiritual merits that make Friday the best day of the week. Understanding this collection allows the faqih to navigate questions about Friday with precision, distinguishing between general fiqh rules and those specific to this blessed day.

Guided Path
1
List the obligatory elements of Friday prayer
Identify the obligations (wajibat) and conditions (shurut) for Friday prayer: obligation of attendance, condition of congregation (minimum 3 men besides imam), condition of khutbah before prayer, recitation of a specific surah, and prohibition of travel when obligated.
Note that the khutbah is a condition (shart) for validity, not a pillar (rukn).
2
Identify the recommended sunan
List the recommended acts: ghusl, perfume, best clothes, trimming nails, shaving hair (but after Friday is better), incense, early attendance, engaging in worship until khatib appears.
Shaving hair after Friday is better than before according to some authorities.
3
Recognize the disliked acts (makruhat)
Note the disliked actions: singling out Friday for fasting, singling out its night for night prayer (qiyam), and delaying prayer for cooling (ibrad) unlike Zuhr.
Fasting Friday alone is makruh tanzihi; combining with Thursday or Saturday removes the dislike.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on midday prayer
According to Abu Yusuf, supererogatory prayer at midday (when sun is at zenith) is not disliked on Friday; this is the mufta bihi view.
Other scholars (including Imam Muhammad) considered it makruh based on a hadith prohibiting prayer at that time.
5
Comprehend the virtues of Friday
Friday is the best day, a day of Eid, contains an hour of acceptance, spirits of the dead meet, graves are visited, deceased are safe from punishment, Hell is not stoked, Adam was created and expelled, the Hour occurs, and Paradise inhabitants visit Allah.
These virtues inform recommended practices like reciting Surah al-Kahf and visiting graves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في أحكام يوم الجمعة اختص بأحكام : لزوم صلاة الجمعة واشتراط الجماعة لها، وكونها ثلاثة سوى الإمام والخطبة لها وكونها قبلها ،شرط وقراءة السورة المخصوصة، وتحريم السفر قبلها بشرطه واستنان الغسل لها والطيب ولبس الأحسن، وتقليم الأظفار وحلق الشعر، ولكن بعدها أفضل والبخور في المسجد والتبكير لها، والاشتغال بالعبادة إلى خروج الخطيب ولا يسنّ الإبراد (۱) بها، ويُكره إفراده بالصوم، وإفراد ليلته بالقيام، وقراءة سورة الكهف فيه ونفي كراهة النافلة وقت الاستواء على قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله المصحح المعتمد وهو خير أيام الأسبوع، ويوم عيد، وفيه ساعة إجابة، وتجتمع فيه الأرواح ، وتُزار فيه القبور ويأمن الميت فيه من عذاب القبر، ومن مات فيه أو في ليلته أمِنَ من فتنة القبر وعذابه، ولا تسجر فيه جهنم، وفيه خلق آدم وفيه أخرج من الجنة، وفيه تقوم الساعة وفيه يزور أهل الجنة ربهم سبحانه وتعالى. وهذا آخر ما أوردناه من فن الجمع والفرق مما يكثر دوره ويقبح بالفقيه جهله، ولله الحمد والمنة وله الحول والقوة. ثم الآن نشرع بحول الله تعالى وقوته في الفرق.
The chapter on the rulings of Friday. It is distinguished by rulings: obligatoriness of Friday prayer, the condition of congregation for it, that (the minimum number) is three besides the imam, the sermon for it and that it is before the prayer, a condition, and reciting the specific surah, prohibition of traveling before it with its condition, recommendation of ghusl for it, perfume, wearing the best clothes, trimming nails, and shaving hair, but after it is better, incense in the mosque, arriving early for it, engaging in worship until the khatib comes out, and delaying (prayer) until cooling is not recommended. It is disliked to single out Friday with fasting, or its night with night prayer. Reciting Surat al-Kahf on it is (recommended), and negation of dislike of supererogatory prayer at midday according to the correct relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him). It is the best day of the week, a day of Eid, it contains an hour of response, spirits meet, graves are visited, and the deceased are safe from the punishment of the grave. Whoever dies on it or its night is safe from the trial and punishment of the grave. Hell is not stoked on it. Adam was created on it, taken out of Paradise on it, the Hour will be established on it, and the inhabitants of Paradise will visit their Lord on it. This is the end of what we have presented from the art of jama' and farq that recurs frequently and whose ignorance is repudiated for the jurist. Praise and favour belong to Allah, and to Him is power and strength. Now we will begin, with Allah's help and strength, on the farq (distinctions).
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings and virtues of Friday (Yawm al-Jumu'ah). It lists specific rulings: the obligation of Friday prayer with the condition of congregation (minimum three men besides the imam according to Hanafi), the sermon (khutbah) as a condition before the prayer, recitation of a specific surah (al-Jumu'ah or al-Munafiqun), prohibition of traveling before it with conditions (if one is obligated to attend), and recommended acts: ghusl, perfume, best clothes, trimming nails, shaving hair (but after Friday is better), incense, arriving early, engaging in worship until the khatib appears. Delaying the prayer to avoid heat is not recommended. It is disliked to single out Friday for fasting or its night for night prayer (qiyam). Reciting Surah al-Kahf is recommended. According to the correct view of Abu Yusuf, supererogatory prayer at midday (when the sun is at zenith) is not disliked. The passage then enumerates the merits of Friday: it is the best day, a day of 'Eid, it contains an hour of acceptance, spirits of the dead meet, visiting graves is recommended, the deceased are safe from grave punishment, especially those who die on Friday or its night. Hell is not stoked on Friday. Adam was created and expelled on Friday, the Hour will come on Friday, and the inhabitants of Paradise will visit Allah on Friday. The passage concludes by marking the end of the section on jama' (combination) and farq (differentiation) from the book Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, and announces the upcoming section on farq.

Key Terms
الجمعة (al-jumu'ah): Friday, the day of congregational prayer and special devotional acts
الإبراد (al-ibrad): Delaying the Friday prayer until the extreme heat subsides
الكهف (al-kahf): Surah al-Kahf (Chapter 18) recited on Friday for blessings

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Obligation of Friday prayer with congregation
B) Recommendation of ghusl and perfume
C) Dislike of fasting on Friday alone
D) Permissibility of traveling before Friday prayer unconditionally

Key Takeaway
Friday is a day of unique obligations and great virtues; the mufti must know both the rulings and the merits to guide the community.

Hierarchy of Mosque Sanctity
A concluding listing of mosque sanctity levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven levels of mosque sanctity in order and state the fiqh ruling that only the top three mosques may be traveled to for extra reward.

Core Idea
Mosque sanctity increases from home prayer rooms to al-Masjid al-Haram; only the first three justify travel.

This concluding passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a concise hierarchy of mosques based on their degree of sanctity (hurmah). The ranking has direct implications for practical rulings such as the virtue of prayer, the permissibility of traveling to a mosque, and the severity of committing offenses within them. Memorizing this hierarchy is essential for any mufti applying related qawa'id.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Starting Point
The phrase 'أعظم المساجد حرمة' indicates the highest sanctity. Note the first mosque listed: al-Masjid al-Haram.
2
Order the Three Sacred Mosques
Recognize 'المسجد الحرام', 'مسجد المدينة', 'مسجد بيت المقدس' as the top three, in that order.
These three are the only ones for which travel (shadd al-rihal) is recommended.
3
List the Remaining Categories
After the first three, note 'الجوامع' (congregational mosques), then 'مساجد المحال' (neighborhood mosques), then 'مساجد الشوارع' (street mosques), then 'مساجد البيوت' (private prayer rooms).
The order is descending in sanctity.
4
Connect to Fiqh Rulings
For each level, recall that the reward for prayer increases with sanctity, and travel is only encouraged for the first three. Also, the sin of transgression is greater in higher ones.
e.g., one prayer in al-Masjid al-Haram equals 100,000 prayers elsewhere.
5
Apply as a Qa'ida
Recognize this as a minor (sughra) nathr qa'ida that can be used in iftaa to prioritize mosque selection and behavior.
This qa'ida is a tool for the mufti, not a source of new rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة : أعظم المساجد حرمة المسجد الحرام ثم مسجد المدينة ثم مسجد بيت المقدس ثم الجوامع ثم مساجد المحال ثم مساجد الشوارع ثم مساجد البيوت .
Conclusion: The greatest mosques in sanctity are: the Sacred Mosque (al-Masjid al-Haram), then the Mosque of the Prophet in Medina, then the Mosque of Jerusalem (al-Aqsa), then the congregational mosques (jami'), then the neighborhood mosques, then the street mosques, then the private prayer rooms (masajid al-buyut).
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This brief conclusion establishes a clear hierarchy of mosques based on their sanctity (hurmah). The highest is the Masjid al-Haram in Mecca, followed by the Prophet's Mosque in Medina, then al-Aqsa in Jerusalem. After these three great sanctuaries, the order is: major congregational mosques (jami') where Friday prayers are held, then local neighborhood mosques, then street mosques (perhaps open spaces used for prayer), and finally private prayer rooms within homes. This ranking affects rulings such as the virtue of prayer, the prohibition of traveling to visit them (only three mosques are to be traveled to), and the severity of committing sins therein.

Key Terms
المسجد الحرام (al-Masjid al-Haram): The Sacred Mosque in Mecca, the holiest site in Islam.
مسجد المدينة (Masjid al-Madinah): The Prophet's Mosque in Medina, the second holiest mosque.
مسجد بيت المقدس (Masjid Bayt al-Maqdis): Al-Aqsa Mosque in Jerusalem, the third holiest site.
الجوامع (al-Jawami'): Congregational mosques where Friday prayers are held.
مساجد المحال (Masajid al-Mahall): Neighborhood mosques serving a local area.
مساجد الشوارع (Masajid al-Shawari'): Street mosques or small prayer spaces along roads.
مساجد البيوت (Masajid al-Buyut): Private prayer rooms within homes, the lowest in sanctity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of mosque sanctity is a key Hanafi qa'ida affecting travel, reward, and legal propriety.

Differences Between Wudu and Ghusl
Contrasting rulings of wudu and ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five key differences between wudu and ghusl according to the Hanafi school and apply the rulings correctly in practice.

Core Idea
What is sunnah in wudu may be fard in ghusl: renewal, order, mouth-rinse, and head-wiping differ.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a concise list of five legal differences between wudu and ghusl. As a mufti, understanding these distinctions is essential to avoid conflating the two. The passage highlights how a single act (e.g., rinsing the mouth) can shift from sunnah to fard depending on the type of impurity being removed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Points of Difference
Read the passage carefully and list each point where the ruling for wudu differs from that for ghusl.
The passage explicitly enumerates: tajdid (renewal), masah al-khuff (wiping over khuffs), tartib (order), madmadah/istinshaq (mouth rinse and sniffing), and wiping the head.
2
Determine the Hukm for Each Act in Wudu and Ghusl
For each point, note the specific legal category (sunnah, makruh, fard, etc.) as stated for both acts.
Pay attention to contrasts: e.g., tajdid al-wudu is sunnah, tajdid al-ghusl is makruh; madmadah is sunnah in wudu but fard in ghusl.
3
Analyze the Shift in Legal Classification
Identify which acts upgrade from sunnah to fard or change in desirability.
Madmadah/istinshaq shifts from sunnah (wudu) to fard (ghusl); tartib is sunnah in wudu and not required in ghusl.
4
Understand the Rationale Underlying Each Difference
Reflect on why the rulings differ. For example, ghusl requires complete washing of the body, making mouth rinse obligatory, while in wudu it is only sunnah.
The foundational principle is that ghusl aims for full purification (ghusl), whereas wudu is a lighter purification.
5
Apply the Rulings to a Practical Scenario
Consider a case: A person renews wudu after changing assembly (sunnah) vs. renewing ghusl after changing assembly (makruh). State the correct mufta bihi.
This exercise reinforces the distinction and prepares you for real fatwa questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الوضوء والغسل : يسن تجديد الوضوء عند اختلاف المجلس، ويُكره تجديد الغسل مطلقا، فيه الخُفَّ وينزع للغسل يسنّ فيه الترتيب بخلاف الغسل، تسنّ المضمضة والاستنشاق فيه بخلاف الغسل ففريضة، تمسح الرأس فيه بخلاف الغسل على قول.
The differences between wudu and ghusl: Renewal of wudu is sunnah when the assembly changes, while renewal of ghusl is disliked absolutely. In it [wudu], wiping the khuff is allowed, but for ghusl one must remove them. Order (tartib) is sunnah in wudu, unlike ghusl. Rinsing the mouth and sniffing water are sunnah in wudu, but in ghusl they are obligatory. Wiping the head is [done] in wudu, unlike ghusl according to one opinion.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage compares wudu and ghusl in five points. First, renewing wudu is recommended when the place or gathering changes (e.g., after entering a mosque), but renewing ghusl is always disliked. Second, during wudu one may wipe over the khuff (leather socks) without removing them, but for ghusl they must be removed. Third, performing the actions in order (tartib) is sunnah in wudu but not required in ghusl. Fourth, rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and sniffing water (istinshaq) are sunnah in wudu but obligatory (fard) in ghusl. Fifth, in wudu one wipes the head, but in ghusl, according to one view, washing the head is obligatory, not just wiping. The passage highlights that some acts shift in legal classification between the two.

Key Terms
تجديد الوضوء (tajdid al-wudu): Performing wudu again even when one is already in a state of purity; sunnah when the assembly changes.
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth with water as an act of purification; sunnah in wudu, fard in ghusl according to the Hanafi school.
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose; sunnah in wudu, fard in ghusl.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Performing the acts of wudu in sequence; sunnah in wudu, not required in ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which act is sunnah in wudu but fard in ghusl according to the passage?
A) Tartib (order)
B) Wiping the head
C) Rinsing the mouth and sniffing water
D) Renewal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acts of purification shift in legal priority between wudu and ghusl, especially in renewal, order, mouth-rinse, and head-wiping.

Differences Between Wiping the Khuff and Washing the Feet
Contrasting rulings of wiping over khuff vs washing feet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the differences between wiping over khuff and washing feet, identify the ruling on wiping over a usurped khuff, and apply the distinctions in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
Washing the foot is the original; wiping the khuff is a restricted concession.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a concise comparison of rulings between two purificatory acts: wiping over the khuff and washing the feet. While both serve to cleanse the feet in wudu, they differ in time limits, coverage, sunnan, and the effect of janabah. Understanding these distinctions helps a mufti apply the correct ruling when the conditions for khuff wiping are present or absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
Recognize that the passage compares wiping over the khuff (masah al-khuff) and washing the feet (ghusl al-rijl) as alternatives in wudu.
Wiping is a substitute (badal) for washing, not equivalent.
2
Extract each point of difference
From the text, list: time limit, usurped item ruling, triple washing, coverage, effect of janabah, and preference.
3
Determine the hukm for each point
For washing: no time limit, sunnah to wash thrice, must cover entire foot, not invalidated by janabah, preferred. For wiping: time-limited, no triple washing, minimal coverage, invalidated by janabah, lesser than washing.
Use the Arabic keywords from the passage.
4
Note the Shafi'i ikhtilaf
The text reports a Shafi'i view on usurped khuff vs foot. Recognize it as a legitimate difference; the Hanafi position is not provided here.
Acknowledge that this is a transmission; do not assume Hanafi agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه مسح الخُف وغسل الرجل : يتأقت المسح ،دونه، ورأيت في بعض كتب الشافعية يجوز غسل الرجل المغصوبة بلا خلاف ولا يجوز مسح الخُفّ المغصوب ؛ وصورة الرجل المغصوبة أن يستحق قطع رجله فلا يمكن منها يسنّ تثليث الغسل دون المسح. يجب تعميم الرجل دون الخُفْ، لا تنقضه الجنابة بخلاف المسح هو أفضل من المسح لمن رآه .
The differences between wiping the khuff and washing the foot: Wiping is time-limited, washing is not. I saw in some Shafi'i books that it is permissible to wash a usurped foot without disagreement, but it is not permissible to wipe over a usurped khuff. The scenario of a usurped foot is when one is entitled to cut off his foot, so it is not possible. Triple washing is sunnah for the foot but not for wiping. Coverage of the foot is obligatory, not for the khuff. Major impurity (janabah) does not break [the validity of foot washing?] unlike wiping [which is invalidated by janabah]. Washing the foot is better than wiping for one who sees it.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage compares wiping over the khuff (masah al-khuff) with washing the feet (ghusl al-rijl) directly. The first point: wiping has a time limit (one day and night for a resident, three for a traveler), while washing the feet does not. Then it mentions a Shafi'i perspective: washing a usurped foot (i.e., a foot that belongs to another? but the context is unclear) is permissible, but wiping over a usurped khuff is not. The example of a 'usurped foot' is when the person is entitled to have it cut off as a legal punishment? Possibly a misstatement. Next, washing the feet three times is sunnah, but wiping is not done three times. Washing must cover the entire foot, but wiping only requires covering about three fingers. Major ritual impurity (janabah) does not affect the foot washing (it is already washed), but it invalidates the khuff wiping, meaning if one becomes junub, he cannot just renew the wiping? Actually, janabah nullifies the permissibility of wiping over khuff, so one must remove the khuff and wash. Finally, washing the foot is considered better than wiping for those who do not have a valid excuse (like fear of cold), because wiping is a concession.

Key Terms
مسح الخف (masah al-khuff): Wiping over the leather socks with wet hands as a substitute for washing the feet, subject to conditions.
غسل الرجل (ghusl al-rijl): Washing the feet in wudu, which is the original obligation.
جنابة (janabah): A state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on triple washing for wiping over khuff?
A) Sunnah
B) Not prescribed
C) Obligatory
D) Recommended

Key Takeaway
Wiping over khuff is a restricted concession with specific limits; it differs from washing the feet in time, coverage, sunnah, and effect of janabah.

Differences Between Wiping the Head and Wiping the Khuff
Contrasting rulings of wiping the head and khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on complete coverage and repetition for wiping the head and khuff, and apply the principle that substitutes have relaxed conditions.

Core Idea
Head wiping requires sunnah coverage; khuff wiping does not. Repeating three times is disliked only for the khuff.

In Hanafi fiqh, the rulings for wiping the head (masah al-ra's) and wiping the leather socks (masah al-khuff) differ in two important ways. This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir concisely highlights the differences regarding complete coverage (isti'ab) and repeating the wipe three times (tathlith). Understanding these distinctions ensures correct practice in wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
The passage directly compares wiping the head (in wudu) and wiping the khuff (in lieu of washing the feet). Recognize that these are separate acts with distinct rulings.
2
Extract the first difference: isti'ab
The text states it is sunnah to cover the entire head, but not the khuff. This means for the head, the recommended practice is to wipe the whole head; for the khuff, only a portion (three fingers) suffices.
Isti'ab = complete coverage.
3
Extract the second difference: tathlith
Wiping three times (tathlith) is not disliked for the head, but it is disliked (makruh) for the khuff. This indicates that the sunnah is to wipe each once, but repetition is permissible for the head and discouraged for the khuff.
Tathlith = wiping three times.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The head is a primary limb (asl) in wudu, while the khuff is a substitute (badal). Substitutes generally have relaxed requirements. Therefore, full coverage and repetition are not required or recommended for the khuff.
This principle explains the differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه مسح الرأس والخُفّ : يسن استيعاب الرأس دون الخُفّ، لو ثلث مسح الرأس لم يُكره وإن لم يندب الخف. ويُكره التثليث مسح الخف.
The differences between wiping the head and wiping the khuff: It is sunnah to cover the entire head, but not the khuff. If one wipes the head three times, it is not disliked, but for the khuff it is not recommended. It is disliked to wipe the khuff three times.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This short passage highlights two points of difference. First, when wiping the head (in wudu), it is sunnah to cover the entire head with the wet hand (isti'ab). For wiping the khuff, however, it is sufficient to wipe at least the area of three fingers, so there is no requirement to cover the whole khuff. Second, repeating the wiping three times (tathlith) for the head is not disliked; it is permissible. But for the khuff, wiping three times is disliked (makruh), because the prescribed wiping is once. Therefore, the sunnah practice is to wipe the head once thoroughly, and to wipe the khuff once lightly.

Key Terms
الاستيعاب (al-isti'ab): Covering the entire part during ritual purification; here, it is sunnah for head wiping but not for khuff.
التثليث (al-tathlith): Performing an action three times; here, it refers to wiping three times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on wiping the khuff three times?
A) Sunnah
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Forbidden (haram)

Key Takeaway
The head and khuff have different rulings regarding complete coverage and repetition, based on the principle that a substitute (khuff) has relaxed requirements compared to the original limb.

Differences Between Wudu and Tayammum
Contrasting rulings of wudu and tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the distinct rulings of tayammum compared to wudu and apply them to practical scenarios using Hanafi legal maxims.

Core Idea
Tayammum is a limited substitute: face and hands only, requires intention, no renewal without excuse, and suffices for both impurities.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a concise list (rasm) of the key differences between wudu and tayammum in the Hanafi school. Understanding these distinctions is essential for a mufti to correctly apply the rules of purification in situations where water is absent or harmful. The passage can be seen as a set of furu' (sub-cases) governed by the broader maxim that a substitute (badal) takes the rulings of the original except where exceptions are explicitly stated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the passage is contrasting wudu and tayammum. The phrase 'ما افترق فيه' signals that only the points of difference are listed, not shared rulings.
Tayammum is a rukhshah (concession), so its rules often differ from the original wudu.
2
List each difference
Go through each element: (1) parts wiped – face and hands only; (2) permissibility – only with an excuse; (3) intention – required; (4) renewal – not sunnah; (5) triplication – not sunnah; (6) breaking – sunnah when excuse ends; (7) scope – covers both minor and major impurity.
Each point is a separate hukm (ruling) that distinguishes tayammum from wudu.
3
Classify by legal category
Determine which category each ruling falls into: shurut (conditions), arkan (pillars), or sunan (recommendations). For example, intention is a condition (shart) for validity, while breaking tayammum is a sunnah act.
This helps understand the weight of each ruling.
4
Connect to underlying maxims
Relate the differences to Hanafi legal maxims. For instance, the rule 'al-badal lahu hukm al-mubdal' (the substitute takes the ruling of the substituted) explains that tayammum generally mirrors wudu, but the listed points are exceptions due to its nature as a concession. Also, 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease) justifies the reduced scope.
These maxims form the basis for deriving further rules.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a traveler who has no water. He performs tayammum and then finds a small amount of water. According to the passage, it is sunnah to break (naqad) the tayammum and perform wudu if possible. If using water would cause harm, the excuse remains, and tayammum continues to be valid.
This demonstrates how the rules are applied in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الوضوء والتيمم : فيه الحف، يمسح كونه في الوجه واليدين فقط ولا يجوز إلا لعذر، ولا ويفتقر إلى النيَّة ولا يسنّ تجديده ولا تثليثه ويسنّ فيه النقض، ويستوي فيه الحدث الأصغر والأكبر.
The differences between wudu and tayammum: In it [tayammum], there is [wiping] the face and hands only, and it is not permissible except for an excuse. It requires intention (niyyah). Its renewal is not sunnah, nor is doing it three times. It is sunnah to break the tayammum (i.e., to nullify it when the excuse ends). It suffices for both minor and major impurity.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage outlines the key distinctions between wudu and tayammum. Tayammum is a dry purification using earth (dust) when water is unavailable or its use would harm. It involves wiping the face and hands only, not the whole limbs required in wudu. It is only permissible with a valid excuse (such as lack of water or illness). It requires intention (niyyah) from the start. Unlike wudu, it is not recommended to renew tayammum (i.e., perform it again without cause), and it is not done three times. It is sunnah to break (naqad) tayammum when the excuse ends, meaning one should seek water as soon as possible. Tayammum is a substitute for both minor and major impurity; thus, one can pray with it even if junub, but it does not remove impurity permanently.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ritual washing of specific parts of the body with water as a prerequisite for prayer.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform an act of worship.
العذر (al-'udhr): A valid excuse that permits tayammum, such as absence of water or illness preventing its use.
التجديد (al-tajdid): Renewal of tayammum without the excuse ending; not recommended.
التثليث (al-tathlith): Performing an action three times. In wudu, it is sunnah to wash each limb thrice; in tayammum, it is not sunnah.
النقض (al-naqad): Breaking/nullifying tayammum voluntarily when the excuse ends; recommended (sunnah).
الحدث الأصغر (al-hadath al-asghar): Minor impurity that requires wudu (e.g., passing wind, urine, etc.).
الحدث الأكبر (al-hadath al-akbar): Major impurity that requires ghusl (e.g., janabah, menstruation, postpartum bleeding).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on renewing tayammum without a valid reason?
A) Sunnah
B) Obligatory
C) Not sunnah
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
Tayammum has specific rulings distinct from wudu due to its nature as a concession; these include limited wiping, obligatory intention, and no recommended renewal or triplication.

Differences Between Wiping Over a Bandage and Wiping Over Khuff
Contrasting ruling of wiping bandage vs khuff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the key differences between the Hanafi rulings on wiping over a bandage (jabirah) and wiping over leather socks (khuff), and apply these distinctions in fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
Bandage wiping is more flexible than khuff wiping—necessity overrides formality.

Wiping over medical bandages (jabirah) and wiping over leather socks (khuff) are both concessions, but they have distinct rules. This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair enumerates eight differences, highlighting that jabirah is governed by the principle of necessity (darurah) while khuff is a regulated substitute. Understanding these nuances helps a mufti apply the correct ruling in medical and purification contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objects being compared
The passage compares wiping over a bandage (jabirah) and wiping over the khuff. Note the legal context: both are alternatives to washing during wudu, but they have different conditions because one is a necessity and the other is a regular substitute.
Jabirah is applied due to injury; khuff is worn for warmth or modesty.
2
Extract each distinct difference
List the eight differences stated in the passage: 1) Condition of tying/putting on; 2) Combination of washing and wiping; 3) Extent of coverage required; 4) Validity of prayer if wiping is omitted; 5) Time limit for wiping; 6) Effect of falling off; 7) Removal for janabah; 8) Multiple bandages vs multiple khuffs.
Each difference has a clear contrasting rule for jabirah and khuff.
3
Analyze the underlying rationale
For each difference, infer the basis: jabirah is a necessity concession, so conditions are relaxed; khuff is a fixed substitute with stricter conditions. For example, jabirah can be applied without wudu because the need is immediate, while khuff requires purity because it is a regular practice.
The principle of 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' applies more strongly to jabirah.
4
Apply the differences to a fatwa scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a bandage falls off before healing), use the passage to determine the ruling: the wiping remains valid and does not need to be repeated. Contrast with the khuff rule where falling off invalidates the wiping.
Practice with each difference to internalize the distinctions.
5
Summarize the legal principle
The overarching qa'ida: 'Necessity (darurah) removes the strictness of conditions, whereas a regulated substitute (badal) retains formalities.' This explains why jabirah has fewer restrictions.
This principle is key for fatwa in medical contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه مسح الجبيرة ومسح الخُفَّ : لا يشترط شدّها على وضوء ويشترط لبسه على كمال الطهارة، وتجمع مع الحف، الغسل بخلاف مسح ويجب تعميمها أو أكثرها بخلاف الخُفّ، وتصح الصلاة بدونه في رواية وهو المعتمد بخلاف المسح على الخُفْ إن لم يغسلهما، ولا يقدر بمدة بخلافه، ولا ينتقض إذا سقطت من غير برء ; فلا تجب إعادته بخلاف الخُفّ سقط لا تنزع للجنابة بخلاف الخُفّ، وإذا كان على عضو جبيرتان فسقطت إحداهما أعادها بلا إعادة مسحها بخلاف نزع أحد الخُفَّين.
The differences between wiping the bandage (jabirah) and wiping the khuff: It is not required that the bandage be tied in a state of wudu, while it is required that the khuff be put on in a state of complete purity. It combines [the ruling of] washing with wiping, unlike the khuff. It is obligatory to cover most of the bandage, unlike the khuff. The prayer is valid without it in one narration, which is the relied-upon view, unlike the khuff where prayer is not valid if the feet are not washed. It is not limited by a time period, unlike the khuff. It is not invalidated if it falls off before healing, so its repetition is not required, unlike the khuff if it falls off. It is not removed due to janabah, unlike the khuff. If there are two bandages on a limb and one falls, he re-applies it without re-wiping, unlike the removal of one khuff.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage compares two types of wiping: over a bandage (jabirah) applied to a wound, and over leather socks (khuff). It enumerates several differences. First, a bandage does not need to be put on while in a state of purity; it can be applied at any time. But the khuff must be put on after performing wudu. Second, with a bandage, one wipes over it but also must wash the healthy parts nearby (it combines washing and wiping), whereas with the khuff, only wiping is done. Third, for the bandage, it is obligatory to wipe at least most of it, whereas for the khuff, wiping a small part (like three fingers) suffices. Fourth, according to a relied-upon narration, prayer performed without wiping the bandage (if forgotten) is valid, but prayer without wiping the khuff is invalid if the feet are not washed. Fifth, the bandage wiping has no time limit; it lasts until healing. Sixth, if the bandage falls off before the wound heals, the previous wiping does not break, so one does not need to repeat the wiping. But if the khuff falls off, it nullifies the wiping and one must wash the feet. Seventh, janabah does not necessitate removing the bandage (one can wipe over it while in janabah), but janabah invalidates the khuff wiping. Eighth, if there are two bandages and one falls, one re-applies it without re-wiping; but if one khuff is removed, both must be removed or the wiping is invalid. These distinctions emphasize the different legal natures: khuff wiping is a full substitute requiring specific conditions, while bandage wiping is a necessity accommodation with fewer conditions.

Key Terms
الجبيرة (al-jabirah): A bandage or splint applied to a wound or fracture; wiping over it is permitted as a necessity.
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet; wiping over them is a substitute for washing during wudu.
الجنابة (al-janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a bandage falls off before the wound heals?
A) The wiping becomes invalid and must be redone.
B) The previous wiping remains valid and no repetition is required.
C) The wound must be washed directly without a bandage.
D) The bandage must be re-applied and the wiping repeated.

Key Takeaway
Bandage wiping is significantly more lenient than khuff wiping due to the principle of necessity.

Differences Between Menstruation and Postpartum Bleeding
Contrasting rulings of menstruation and postpartum bleeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal distinctions between hayd and nifas regarding duration, legal capacity (bulugh/istibra'), and effect on consecutive fasting, and apply these distinctions in issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
Menstruation and postpartum bleeding differ in minimum duration, legal capacity, and interruption of consecutive fasting.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) in several key rulings. While both share some legal effects, such as preventing prayer and fasting, this passage highlights specific differences in duration, their role in establishing puberty and istibra', and their impact on the continuity of expiatory fasting. Understanding these nuances is essential for a mufti when responding to questions about women's fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the points of contrast
Read the passage and list the three distinct areas where hayd and nifas are treated differently: minimum/maximum duration, legal capacity, and effect on consecutive fasting.
The passage explicitly contrasts these points in a concise manner.
2
Analyze the duration rules
Note that hayd has a minimum (3 days, established elsewhere in Hanafi fiqh) and a maximum of 10 days. Nifas has no minimum and a maximum of 40 days. This affects when a woman considers herself in a state of purity.
The text says 'أقل الحيض محدود' implying a defined minimum; the standard Hanafi minimum is three days.
3
Understand legal capacity
The passage states that puberty (bulugh) and istibra' (ascertaining the womb's emptiness after divorce or death) occur through hayd, not nifas. This means only hayd can determine that a woman has reached puberty or that her iddah is complete.
Istibra' is used for divorced women or widows to ensure no pregnancy.
4
Apply the fasting rule
The text asserts that hayd does not break the continuity (tawatur) required in expiatory fasting (kaffarah). This implies that a woman may pause during her period and resume without penalty. The rule for nifas is not mentioned, but a mufti must determine if it applies by analogy.
The standard Hanafi position extends this ruling to nifas as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الحيض والنفاس : أقل الحيض محدود ولا حد لأقل النفاس وأكثره عشرة وأكثر النفاس أربعون ويكون به البلوغ والاستبراء دون النفاس والحيض لا يقطع التتابع في صوم الكفارة
The differences between menstruation and postpartum bleeding: The minimum menstruation is limited, and there is no limit to the minimum postpartum bleeding. The maximum menstruation is ten days, and the maximum postpartum is forty days. Puberty (bulugh) and istibra' (ascertaining the womb's innocence) occur through menstruation, not through postpartum bleeding. Menstruation does not break the continuity (tawatur) in fasting expiation.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between hayd (menstruation) and nifas (postpartum bleeding) on several points. First, the minimum duration of menstruation is defined (in the Hanafi school, three days), but there is no minimum for nifas (it can be a day or less). The maximum for menstruation is ten days, while the maximum for nifas is forty days. Second, menstruation is a sign of puberty (bulugh) and is used to ascertain istibra' (that the womb is free from pregnancy after divorce or death of husband), but nifas does not serve that purpose. Third, in the case of fasting as expiation (such as for breaking an oath), if a woman misses days due to menstruation, she can continue the sequence after her period; menstruation does not break the consecutive fasting requirement (tawatur). However, for nifas, the ruling is different? The text doesn't specify for nifas, but it implies that menstruation is different in this regard.

Key Terms
النفاس (al-nifas): The bleeding that occurs after childbirth, with a maximum duration of forty days in Hanafi fiqh.
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual bleeding that occurs in a healthy, non-pregnant woman, with a minimum of three days and a maximum of ten days.
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period to ascertain that the womb is free from pregnancy after divorce or death of husband.
التتابع (al-tawatur): Consecutiveness, especially in fasting expiation (kaffarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ten days
B) Forty days
C) Sixty days
D) It is unlimited

Key Takeaway
Menstruation and postpartum bleeding share some rulings but differ in minimum duration, legal capacity, and effect on consecutive expiatory fasting.
