Distinctions Between Wudu and Ghusl
Lists five differences between wudu and ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific differences between wudu and ghusl according to the Hanafi school, and apply these distinctions when deriving fatwa.

Core Idea
Though wudu and ghusl both purify, their rulings differ on renewal, order, mouth-rinsing, head-wiping, and khuff removal.

A mufti must know not only the rules of each act but also the precise points where similar acts diverge. This passage from a furuq text enumerates the five key differences between wudu and ghusl, helping you avoid conflating their subsidiary rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
Note that the comparison is between ritual ablution (wudu) and full-body wash (ghusl). Both remove impurity but have different conditions.
2
List each stated distinction
For each point in the text, write the ruling for wudu and the contrasting ruling for ghusl.
Use the key terms: sunnah, fard, makruh, mustahab.
3
Categorize the type of difference
Determine whether the difference relates to obligation (fard vs sunnah), timing (renewal), or manner (order, wiping).
This helps in qiyas and tafriq.
4
Derive the underlying principle (if any)
Ask: why does the ruling differ? Often it relates to the nature of the impurity (minor vs major) or the level of difficulty.
Not explicitly stated, but useful for fatwa reasoning.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical situation (e.g., someone renews ghusl when changing assembly), use these distinctions to rule correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الوضوء والغسل :
• يمسح [؟] يسن تجديد الوضوء عند اختلاف المجلس، ويُكره تجديد الغسل مطلقا، فيه الخُفَّ وينزع للغسل يسنّ فيه الترتيب بخلاف الغسل، تسنّ المضمضة والاستنشاق فيه بخلاف الغسل ففريضة، تمسح الرأس فيه بخلاف الغسل على قول.

ما افترق فيه مسح الخُف وغسل الرجل :
يتأقت المسح ،دونه، ورأيت في بعض كتب الشافعية يجوز غسل الرجل المغصوبة بلا خلاف ولا يجوز مسح الخُفّ المغصوب ؛ وصورة الرجل المغصوبة أن يستحق قطع رجله فلا يمكن منها يسنّ تثليث الغسل دون المسح. يجب تعميم الرجل دون الخُفْ، لا تنقضه الجنابة بخلاف المسح هو أفضل من المسح لمن رآه .

ما افترق فيه مسح الرأس والخُفّ :
، يسن استيعاب الرأس دون الخُفّ، لو ثلث مسح الرأس لم يُكره وإن لم يندب الخف. ويُكره التثليث مسح الخف.

ما افترق فيه الوضوء والتيمم :
فيه الحف، يمسح كونه في الوجه واليدين فقط ولا يجوز إلا لعذر، ولا ويفتقر إلى النيَّة ولا يسنّ تجديده ولا تثليثه ويسنّ فيه النقض، ويستوي فيه الحدث الأصغر والأكبر.

ما افترق فيه مسح الجبيرة ومسح الخُفَّ :
لا يشترط شدّها على وضوء ويشترط لبسه على كمال الطهارة، وتجمع مع الحف، الغسل بخلاف مسح ويجب تعميمها أو أكثرها بخلاف الخُفّ، وتصح الصلاة بدونه في رواية وهو المعتمد بخلاف المسح على الخُفْ إن لم يغسلهما، ولا يقدر بمدة بخلافه، ولا ينتقض إذا سقطت من غير برء ؛ فلا تجب إعادته بخلاف الخُفّ سقط لا تنزع للجنابة بخلاف الخُفّ، وإذا كان على عضو جبيرتان فسقطت إحداهما أعادها بلا إعادة مسحها بخلاف نزع أحد الخُفَّين.

ما افترق فيه الحيض والنفاس :
أقل الحيض محدود ولا حد لأقل النفاس وأكثره عشرة وأكثر النفاس أربعون ويكون به البلوغ والاستبراء دون النفاس والحيض لا يقطع التتابع في صوم الكفارة بخلاف النفاس، وتنقضي العدة به دون النفاس، ويحصل به الفصل بين طلاقي السنة والبدعة بخلاف النفاس. فهي سبعة؛ فما في النهاية من الافتراق بأربعة قصور.

ما افترق فيه الأذان والإقامة:
يجوز تراخي الصلاة عن الأذان دون الإقامة يسنّ التمهيل فيه والإسراع فيها، تكره إقامة المحدث لا أذانه ويُكره التكرار فيها لا فيه .

ما افترق فيه سجود السهو والتلاوة:
هو سجدتان وهي واحدة هو في آخر صلاته بعد السلام وهي فيها، هو لا يتكرر بخلافها، لا يقوم له ويقوم لها يتشهد له ويسلم بخلافها، الذكر المشروع في سجود التلاوة لا يشرع فيه .

ما افترق فيه سجود التلاوة والشكر:
سجود الشكر لا يدخل الصلاة بخلافها، واتفقوا على وجوب سجدة التلاوة بخلاف سجدة الشكر. فإنها جائزة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا واجبة، وهو معنى ما عنها أنها ليست مشروعة؛ أي وجوبًا.

ما افترق فيه الإمام والمأموم:
نيَّة الائتمام واجبة على المأموم دون الإمام إلا لصحة صلاة النساء خلفه أو لحصول الفضيلة، ولا تبطل صلاة الإمام إذا بطلت صلاة المأموم بخلاف عكسه، إذا عين الإمام وأخطأ لم يصح اقتداؤه بخلاف الإمام إذا عين المأموم وأخطأ.

ما افترق فيه الجمعة والعيد:
الجمعة فرض والعيد واجب وقتها وقت الظهر ووقته بعد طلوع الشمس إلى زوالها، وشرطها الخطبة وكونها قبلها بخلافه فيهما، وأن لا تعدّد في مصر على مرجوح ،بخلافه، ويستحب في عيد الفطر أن يطعم قبل خروجه إلى المصلى بخلافها.

ما افترق فيه غسل الميت والحي:
تستحب البداية بغسل وجه الميت بخلاف الحي؛ فإنه يبدأ بغسل يديه ولا يمضمض ولا يستنشق بخلاف الحي، ولا يؤخر غسل رجليه بخلاف الحي إن كان في مستنقع الماء، ولا يمسح رأسه في وضوء الغسل بخلاف الحي في رواية .
What distinguishes wudu and ghusl: It is sunna to renew wudu if the gathering changes, and it is disliked to renew ghusl absolutely. Wiping over khuff is in wudu and removed for ghusl. Order is sunna in wudu unlike ghusl. Rinsing the mouth and nose are sunna in wudu, obligatory in ghusl. Wiping the head is sunna in wudu, not in ghusl according to a narration.

What distinguishes wiping over khuff and washing the foot: Wiping is time-limited, washing is not. I saw in some Shafi'i books that it is permissible to wash a usurped foot without disagreement, but not to wipe over a usurped khuff. The case of a usurped foot is when one deserves cutting off his foot, so he cannot [wash]. It is sunna to wash three times without wiping. It is obligatory to cover the foot entirely unlike the khuff. Janabah does not nullify [washing] unlike wiping. Washing is better than wiping for one who sees it.

What distinguishes wiping the head and wiping the khuff: It is sunna to cover the entire head, not the khuff. If one wipes the head three times, it is not disliked though not recommended. It is disliked to wipe the khuff three times.

What distinguishes wudu and tayammum: In it [tayammum] is [wiping] the face and hands only, and it is only permitted for an excuse. It requires intention. It is not sunna to renew it nor to do it three times. It is sunna to nullify it. The minor and major hadath are equal in it.

What distinguishes wiping over bandage and wiping over khuff: It is not required to tie the bandage in a state of wudu, but it is required to put on the khuff in complete purity. Washing is combined with wiping over the bandage unlike wiping over khuff. It is obligatory to cover most of the bandage unlike the khuff. Prayer is valid without it in one narration, and that is relied upon, unlike wiping over khuff if he does not wash his feet. It is not limited by a time period unlike khuff. It is not nullified if it falls off without healing, so its repetition is not required unlike the khuff if it falls. It is not removed for janabah unlike the khuff. If there are two bandages on a limb and one falls, he replaces it without repeating its wipe, unlike removing one of the two khuffs.

What distinguishes menstruation and postpartum bleeding: The minimum of menstruation is limited, the minimum of postpartum is not limited. Its maximum is ten days, and the maximum of postpartum is forty. Puberty and istibra' (waiting) occur with menstruation, not postpartum. Menstruation does not break continuity in fasting of expiation unlike postpartum. The 'iddah ends with menstruation, not postpartum. It distinguishes between the two divorces of sunna and bid'a, unlike postpartum. So they are seven; what is in al-Nihaya of four differences is deficient.

What distinguishes adhan and iqama: It is permissible to delay prayer after adhan, not after iqama. Slowness is sunna in adhan, speed in iqama. It is disliked for a person in hadath to perform iqama, not adhan. Repetition is disliked in iqama, not in adhan.

What distinguishes prostration of forgetfulness and recitation: The former is two prostrations, the latter is one. The former comes at the end of his prayer after salam, the latter during it. The former does not repeat, the latter does. One does not stand for the former but stands for the latter. The former includes tashahhud and salam, the latter does not. The prescribed dhikr in prostration of recitation is not prescribed in the former.

What distinguishes prostration of recitation and gratitude: The prostration of gratitude does not enter the prayer, unlike it. They agreed on the obligation of prostration of recitation, unlike prostration of gratitude, which is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, not obligatory. That is what is meant by it not being prescribed—i.e., not obligatory.

What distinguishes the imam and the follower: Intention of following is obligatory for the follower, not for the imam, except for the validity of women's prayer behind him or for obtaining virtue. The imam's prayer is not invalidated if the follower's prayer is invalidated, unlike the opposite. If the imam specifies [someone] and is mistaken, his following is not valid, unlike the imam if he specifies the follower and is mistaken.

What distinguishes Friday and Eid: Friday is fard, Eid is wajib. Its time is the time of zuhr, its time is after sunrise to zawal. Its condition is the khutbah and that it be before it, unlike both. And that it not be multiple in a city according to the weaker view, unlike it. It is recommended in Eid al-Fitr to eat before going to the prayer ground, unlike Friday.

What distinguishes washing the deceased and the living: It is recommended to start with washing the face of the deceased unlike the living; he starts with washing his hands. He does not rinse his mouth or nose unlike the living. He does not delay washing his feet unlike the living if he is in a pool of water. He does not wipe his head in the wudu of ghusl unlike the living in one narration.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a comparative fiqh compilation from the Hanafi school, listing the specific distinctions between pairs of related legal acts. Each section begins with 'ما افترق فيه' (what distinguishes) and enumerates multiple points of difference. The author systematically contrasts rulings on wudu and ghusl, wiping over khuff and washing feet, head wiping and khuff wiping, wudu and tayammum, bandage wiping and khuff wiping, menstruation and postpartum, adhan and iqama, prostrations, imam and follower, Friday and Eid, and washing the deceased versus the living. The commentary follows the order of the text, highlighting key distinctions such as: in wudu vs ghusl, the number of times, order, and wiping over khuff; in khuff vs foot washing, the time limit and usury issues; in head vs khuff wiping, the coverage and repetition; in wudu vs tayammum, the requirement of intention and equal treatment of major/minor impurity; in bandage vs khuff, the conditions of application and removal; in menstruation vs postpartum, the limits and effects on iddah and divorce; in adhan vs iqama, the timing and recitation norms; in prostrations, the number and position; in imam/follower, the intention and effect of invalidity; in Friday/Eid, obligation and time; in washing deceased/living, the order and omissions. The passage is a useful reference for students to memorize the specific rules that differentiate similar acts, showing that fiqh is not monolithic but nuanced.

Key Terms
الوضوء (al-wuḍūʾ): Ritual ablution for minor impurity, consisting of washing face, arms, wiping head, washing feet.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Full body wash for major impurity (janabah, menstruation, etc.).
تجديد (tajdīd): Renewal; performing the act again even if still valid.
مسح الخف (masḥ al-khuff): Wiping over leather socks instead of washing feet, permissible in wudu for a limited period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on rinsing the mouth in wudu and in ghusl?
A) Sunnah in both.
B) Fard in wudu, sunnah in ghusl.
C) Sunnah in wudu, fard in ghusl.
D) Makruh in wudu, mustahab in ghusl.

Key Takeaway
Wudu and ghusl share the goal of purity but differ in five specific subsidiary rulings that a mufti must know to issue correct fatwa.

Differences between Adhan and Iqamah
Listing legal differences between adhan and iqamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four differences between adhan and iqamah in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to determine the ruling in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Adhan and iqamah have distinct rulings despite their shared purpose.

The adhan and iqamah are both calls to prayer, but they are not identical in the sight of the Hanafi school. The great text al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir lists four key differences that every mufti must know when issuing fatwas regarding prayer calls. These differences arise because the iqamah is more intimately connected to the actual prayer, imposing stricter rules. In this lesson, we will examine each difference and understand the underlying wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four legal differences
Read the Arabic text and extract each distinct ruling that separates adhan from iqamah. The text uses phrases like 'يجوز تراخي الصلاة عن الأذان دون الإقامة' to indicate each difference.
These are four explicit furuat under the qawida that iqamah is more strongly linked to the prayer.
2
Analyze the rationale behind each difference
For each difference, consider why the iqamah is stricter. For example, iqamah signals immediate prayer, so delay is not allowed; it is closer to the prayer, so purity is more emphasized; it is brief, so repetition is disliked.
The underlying principle is that the iqamah is sunnah adjoining the fard (sunnah tu jawiru al-fard).
3
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Given a practical case (e.g., a person delays prayer after iqamah to give a talk), use the differences to determine the ruling. Justify your answer with the specific difference.
Use the mufti's lens: what is the hukm? Is it makruh, permissible, etc.?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الأذان والإقامة:
يجوز تراخي الصلاة عن الأذان دون الإقامة يسنّ التمهيل فيه والإسراع فيها، تكره إقامة المحدث لا أذانه ويُكره التكرار فيها لا فيه.
What distinguishes the adhan from the iqamah: It is permissible to delay the prayer after the adhan but not after the iqamah; it is recommended to be slow in the adhan and quick in the iqamah; the iqamah of a person in a state of impurity is disliked, not his adhan; and repetition is disliked in the iqamah but not in the adhan.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage enumerates four differences between the adhan and iqamah in Hanafi fiqh. First: delaying the prayer after the adhan is permissible, but after the iqamah it is not, as the iqamah signals immediate commencement. Second: the adhan is recited slowly (tamhil) while the iqamah is recited quickly (isra'). Third: it is disliked (makruh) for a person without wudu to perform the iqamah, but not the adhan, because the iqamah is more closely connected to the prayer. Fourth: repetition of phrases is disliked in the iqamah (e.g., saying 'Allahu Akbar' twice each) but not in the adhan, which normally repeats certain phrases.

Key Terms
الأذان (al-adhan): The call to prayer, announcing the beginning of the prayer time.
الإقامة (al-iqamah): The second call to prayer, recited immediately before the prayer begins.
التكرار (al-tikrar): Repetition of phrases; in the iqamah, it is disliked to repeat phrases beyond the standard single recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a difference between adhan and iqamah?
A) Delaying prayer after adhan is allowed, not after iqamah.
B) Adhan is recited slowly, iqamah quickly.
C) Both adhan and iqamah require wudu.
D) Repetition is disliked in iqamah but not in adhan.

Key Takeaway
The iqamah is more closely tied to the prayer than the adhan, resulting in stricter rulings.

Key Differences Between Sajdat al-Sahw and Sajdat al-Tilawah
Listing differences between two types of prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state at least five differences between sajdat al-sahw and sajdat al-tilawah according to the Hanafi school and apply the correct ruling when a person encounters either scenario.

Core Idea
Sajdat al-sahw and sajdat al-tilawah differ in number, timing, repetition, posture, and post-prostration acts.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair compiles the distinctions between two types of prostration in Hanafi fiqh. Memorizing these differences is essential for correct practice and for answering fatwa queries about prayer prostrations. Let's break down each point.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish number and timing
Note that sajdat al-sahw consists of two prostrations and is performed after the taslim (at the end of prayer), while sajdat al-tilawah is a single prostration performed during the prayer upon reciting the verse.
This is the most fundamental difference.
2
Distinguish repetition
Sajdat al-sahw is not repeated in the same prayer even if multiple forgetfulness occur, but sajdat al-tilawah can be performed each time a prostration verse is recited.
This affects how a mufti advises a person who forgets multiple times.
3
Distinguish posture and required acts
For sajdat al-sahw, one does not stand up after prostrating; for sajdat al-tilawah, one stands up. Additionally, sajdat al-sahw requires tashahhud and salam, while sajdat al-tilawah does not. The prescribed dhikr for tilawah is not recited in sahw.
These details matter for the validity of the actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه سجود السهو والتلاوة:
هو سجدتان وهي واحدة هو في آخر صلاته بعد السلام وهي فيها، هو لا يتكرر بخلافها، لا يقوم له ويقوم لها يتشهد له ويسلم بخلافها، الذكر المشروع في سجود التلاوة لا يشرع فيه.
What distinguishes the prostration of forgetfulness from the prostration of recitation: It (sahw) is two prostrations, while it (tilawah) is one; it (sahw) is at the end of the prayer after the taslim, while it (tilawah) is during it; it (sahw) is not repeated, unlike it (tilawah) (may be repeated); one does not stand up for it (sahw) but stands up for it (tilawah); one recites tashahhud and says taslim for it (sahw) unlike it (tilawah); the prescribed dhikr in prostration of recitation is not prescribed in it (sahw).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage outlines the key differences between sajdat al-sahw (prostration for forgetfulness) and sajdat al-tilawah (prostration upon reciting a verse of prostration) according to the Hanafi school. The sahw consists of two prostrations; tilawah is one. The sahw is performed at the end of the prayer after the salam, while tilawah is performed during the prayer when the verse is recited. The sahw is not repeated in the same prayer if multiple forgetfulness occur, but tilawah can be performed each time a verse is recited. For sahw, one does not stand up again after prostrating; for tilawah, one stands up after prostrating. The sahw requires tashahhud and salam; tilawah does not. The dhikr taught for tilawah (e.g., 'sajada wajhiya') is not prescribed for sahw.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sajdat al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness during prayer.
سجود التلاوة (sajdat al-tilawah): Prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of prostration in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many prostrations are performed in sajdat al-sahw?
A) One
B) Two
C) Four
D) It depends on the type of forgetfulness

Key Takeaway
Sajdat al-sahw and sajdat al-tilawah are distinct acts with different numbers, timings, repetition rules, and post-prostration requirements.

Differences between prostration of recitation and gratitude
Listing differences between sajdat al-tilawah and sajdat al-shukr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two differences between sajdat al-tilawah and sajdat al-shukr, and apply the Hanafi ruling that sajdat al-shukr is permissible (ja'iz) not obligatory.

Core Idea
Sajdat al-shukr is permissible and never performed inside prayer, unlike sajdat al-tilawah.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa Al-Naza'ir presents a concise comparison between two similar acts of worship: the prostration of recitation and the prostration of gratitude. By examining their differences, you will internalise a key maxim about how rulings and conditions vary between acts of prostration in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
Recognise that both are acts of prostration (sajdah) but have different triggers: recitation of a prostration verse vs. experiencing a blessing or relief from harm.
2
Note the first difference: place
Sajdat al-tilawah can be performed during or outside prayer; sajdat al-shukr is never performed inside prayer.
3
Note the second difference: ruling
Sajdat al-tilawah is wajib (obligatory) by consensus; sajdat al-shukr is ja'iz (permissible) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, not wajib.
4
Understand 'laysat mashru'ah'
The phrase 'not prescribed' does not mean prohibited; it means not obligatory. The act itself is still permissible and rewardable.
This is a common source of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه سجود التلاوة والشكر:
سجود الشكر لا يدخل الصلاة بخلافها، واتفقوا على وجوب سجدة التلاوة بخلاف سجدة الشكر. فإنها جائزة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا واجبة، وهو معنى ما عنها أنها ليست مشروعة؛ أي وجوبًا.
What distinguishes the prostration of recitation from the prostration of gratitude: The prostration of gratitude does not enter into the prayer, unlike it (tilawah); and they agreed on the obligation of the prostration of recitation, unlike the prostration of gratitude. For it (shukr) is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, not obligatory. And that is the meaning of what is reported from him that it is not prescribed, i.e., obligatory.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage highlights that sajdat al-shukr (prostration of gratitude) differs from sajdat al-tilawah in two main ways. First, sajdat al-shukr is not performed during prayer; it is only performed outside prayer when one receives a blessing or is saved from calamity. Second, there is a consensus (ittifaq) that sajdat al-tilawah is obligatory (wajib) upon reciting a verse of prostration, while sajdat al-shukr is not obligatory but merely permissible (ja'iz) according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Some reports say it is 'not prescribed' (laysat mashru'ah), meaning not obligatory, but it is still permissible as an act of worship.

Key Terms
سجود الشكر (sajdat al-shukr): Prostration performed as a sign of gratitude to Allah for a specific blessing or relief from harm.
سجود التلاوة (sajdat al-tilawah): Prostration performed upon reciting or hearing a verse of prostration in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of sajdat al-shukr in the Hanafi school?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Permissible (ja'iz)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (mandub)

Key Takeaway
Sajdat al-tilawah is wajib and can be performed in prayer; sajdat al-shukr is ja'iz and performed only outside prayer.

Three Distinctions Between Imam and Follower in Prayer
Listing three legal differences between imam and follower in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state three legal differences between the imam and the follower in Hanafi prayer and apply the underlying qa'ida to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
The follower is bound to the imam; the imam is independent unless women follow or virtue is sought.

This passage from the Ashbah wa al-Nazha'ir lists key furqan (distinctions) between the imam and the ma'mum. These distinctions stem from the broader Hanafi principle that the follower's prayer is subordinate to the imam's, while the imam's prayer is independent. Understanding these differences is essential for muftis when answering questions about congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first furqah – intention
Note that niyyah of following is wajib on the follower, but not on the imam. However, the imam may need to intend leading if women follow or to attain virtue.
Intention is a condition for the follower's validity; for the imam, it is recommended unless women are present.
2
Identify the second furqah – invalidity transfer
If the follower's prayer becomes invalid, the imam's remains valid. But if the imam's prayer becomes invalid, the follower's also becomes invalid because the follower is tied to him.
Asymmetry reflects the follower's dependence on the imam.
3
Identify the third furqah – specification error
If the follower mistakenly specifies the imam (e.g., thinking it is Zaid when it is Amr), his following is invalid. If the imam mistakenly specifies a follower, his imamah remains valid.
The follower must correctly identify the imam; the imam need not know who follows.
4
Extract the underlying qa'ida
The common principle is: the follower is subordinate to the imam (الْمَأْمُومُ تَابِعٌ لِلْإِمَامِ), not vice versa. Exceptions exist for intention.
This qa'ida governs many furu' in congregational prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الإمام والمأموم:
نيَّة الائتمام واجبة على المأموم دون الإمام إلا لصحة صلاة النساء خلفه أو لحصول الفضيلة، ولا تبطل صلاة الإمام إذا بطلت صلاة المأموم بخلاف عكسه، إذا عين الإمام وأخطأ لم يصح اقتداؤه بخلاف الإمام إذا عين المأموم وأخطأ.
What distinguishes the imam and the follower: The intention of following (the imam) is obligatory upon the follower, not upon the imam, except for the validity of women's prayer behind him or for attaining virtue; and the prayer of the imam does not become invalid if the prayer of the follower becomes invalid, unlike the reverse; if the follower specifies the imam and makes a mistake, his following is not valid, unlike the imam if he specifies the follower and makes a mistake.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage enumerates three differences between the imam and the follower (ma'mum) in congregational prayer. First, making the intention (niyyah) to follow the imam is obligatory (wajib) for the follower, but not for the imam (he only needs to intend his own prayer). However, the imam may need to intend leading if the followers are women (so that their prayer is valid) or to attain the virtue of congregation. Second, if the follower's prayer becomes invalid for any reason, the imam's prayer remains valid, but if the imam's prayer becomes invalid, the follower's prayer also becomes invalid because the follower is bound to the imam. Third, if the follower mistakenly specifies a particular imam (e.g., thinking it is Zaid when it is Amr), his following is invalid, but if the imam mistakenly specifies a particular follower (e.g., intending to lead only one person who is not actually there), his imamah is still valid.

Key Terms
نية الائتمام (niyyat al-i'timam): The intention to follow the imam in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the imam mistakenly thinks he is leading only one person but two are behind him, what happens?
A) His prayer becomes invalid.
B) His prayer remains valid.
C) He must repeat the prayer.
D) The followers' prayers become invalid.

Key Takeaway
The follower is dependent on the imam; the imam is independent except in the matter of intention for women or virtue.

Differences Between Friday and Eid Prayers: A Qawida of Distinction
Listing differences as a memorization anchor for a qawida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the five key differences between Jumu'ah and Eid prayers as per the Hanafi school, and recognize the underlying qawida.

Core Idea
Jumu'ah is fard, Eid is wajib; times, khutbah sequence, multiplicity, and pre-Eid meal all differ.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair succinctly lists the differences between Jumu'ah and Eid prayers. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for a mufti to issue correct verdicts regarding these congregational prayers. The list itself functions as a sub-maxim (dabt) for comparing similar acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first difference: legal status
Note that Jumu'ah is fard 'ayn (individual obligation) while Eid is wajib (necessary). This is a fundamental distinction in Hanafi hierarchy.
Memory aid: Jumu'ah replaces Zuhr, so it shares its obligation level.
2
Compare the time windows
Jumu'ah time is the same as Zuhr (from zawal to optimal shadow). Eid time is from sunrise to zawal (noon).
Eid prayers are performed in the early morning.
3
Contrast the khutbah (sermon) timing
For Jumu'ah, the khutbah is a condition (shart) and must precede the prayer. For Eid, the khutbah comes after the prayer.
This is a major procedural difference.
4
Analyse the multiplicity of prayers in a city
Multiple Jumu'ah prayers in one city are impermissible according to the weaker (marjuh) opinion; multiple Eid prayers are permissible. The mufta bihi allows multiple Jumu'ah when needed.
The marjuh opinion is cited to highlight the default rule against multiplicity.
5
Note the recommended pre-prayer meal
It is mustahabb (recommended) to eat before leaving for Eid al-Fitr prayer, but no such recommendation exists for Jumu'ah.
This is linked to the nature of Eid as a celebration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الجمعة والعيد:
الجمعة فرض والعيد واجب وقتها وقت الظهر ووقته بعد طلوع الشمس إلى زوالها، وشرطها الخطبة وكونها قبلها بخلافه فيهما، وأن لا تعدّد في مصر على مرجوح، بخلافه، ويستحب في عيد الفطر أن يطعم قبل خروجه إلى المصلى بخلافها.
What distinguishes Friday prayer from the Eid prayer: Jumu'ah is fard and Eid is wajib; its time is the time of Zuhr and its time is after sunrise to its decline; and its condition is the khutbah and that it (khutbah) is before it (prayer), unlike them (i.e., in Eid the khutbah is after prayer); and that there should not be multiple (prayers) in a city according to the weaker opinion, unlike it (i.e., multiple Jumu'ahs are not allowed); and it is recommended in Eid al-Fitr to eat before going out to the prayer ground, unlike Jumu'ah.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage outlines several differences between Jumu'ah (Friday prayer) and Salat al-Eid (Eid prayer). Jumu'ah is a fard (obligatory), while Eid is a wajib (necessary but slightly less than fard in the Hanafi hierarchy). Jumu'ah time is the same as Zuhr (from midday to the shadow of an object equal to its height), while Eid time is from sunrise to before noon (zawal). The khutbah (sermon) is a condition (shart) for Jumu'ah and must be before the prayer; for Eid, the khutbah is after the prayer. According to the weaker (marjuh) opinion, multiple Jumu'ah prayers are not allowed in one city, while multiple Eid prayers may be allowed. It is recommended (mustahabb) to eat before going to the prayer ground for Eid al-Fitr, but not for Jumu'ah.

Key Terms
الجمعة (al-Jumu'ah): The Friday congregational prayer, replacing Zuhr prayer for those obligated.
العيد (al-'Id): The two annual festival prayers (Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha).
الخطبة (khutbah): The sermon delivered during Jumu'ah and Eid prayers.
مرجوح (marjuh): The weaker or less preferred opinion in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the khutbah (sermon) in Eid prayer according to this passage?
A) It is before the prayer, like Jumu'ah
B) It is after the prayer
C) There is no khutbah in Eid
D) It is optional

Key Takeaway
Jumu'ah and Eid prayers differ in five key aspects: hukm, time, khutbah position, multiplicity, and pre-prayer eating.

Four Differences Between Ghusl of the Deceased and Ghusl of the Living
Listing four differences between ghusl of the deceased and ghusl of the living

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four differences between ghusl al-mayyit and ghusl al-hayy and apply the underlying maxim to identify further differences.

Core Idea
Washing the deceased: start with face, skip mouth/nose, wash feet early, no head wipe in wudu.

Washing a deceased person (ghusl al-mayyit) is a communal obligation (fard kifayah) with specific procedures that differ from the ritual bath (ghusl) of a living person. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair lists four key differences that are essential for anyone performing or teaching this rite. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion and ensures correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first difference: order of washing
For the deceased, it is recommended to start with the face; for the living, the hands come first.
2
Identify the second difference: mouth and nose rinsing
The deceased is not given mouth rinse (madmadah) or nose rinse (istinshaq), while the living does as part of wudu/ghusl.
3
Identify the third difference: timing of foot washing
For the deceased, wash the feet immediately without delay; for a living person in a pool, delay is permissible.
4
Identify the fourth difference: head wiping in wudu of ghusl
According to one narration, the deceased does not receive head wiping; the living does. Note the phrase 'in one narration' indicates a disputed point.
5
Recognize the ikhtilaf
The fourth difference has a variant narration; be aware that there is an intra-Hanafi difference of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه غسل الميت والحي:
تستحب البداية بغسل وجه الميت بخلاف الحي؛ فإنه يبدأ بغسل يديه ولا يمضمض ولا يستنشق بخلاف الحي، ولا يؤخر غسل رجليه بخلاف الحي إن كان في مستنقع الماء، ولا يمسح رأسه في وضوء الغسل بخلاف الحي في رواية.
What distinguishes washing the deceased from washing the living: It is recommended to start by washing the face of the deceased, unlike the living; for the living starts by washing his hands. And one does not rinse the mouth or nose of the deceased, unlike the living. And one does not delay washing his feet, unlike the living if he is in a pool of water. And one does not wipe his head in the ablution of the bath (ghusl), unlike the living, according to one narration.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents differences between ghusl of a deceased person (ghusl al-mayyit) and ghusl of a living person (ghusl al-hayy) performing ritual purification. Four differences are listed: (1) It is recommended (mustahabb) to start by washing the face of the deceased, whereas a living person performing ghusl starts by washing the hands. (2) The deceased is not given mouth rinse (madmadah) or nose rinse (istinshaq), while a living person does these as part of wudu/ghusl. (3) The feet of the deceased are not delayed; they are washed immediately, whereas a living person in a pool of water may delay washing the feet until the end. (4) According to one narration (riwayah), one does not wipe the head of the deceased as part of the 'wudu of ghusl', unlike a living person who wipes the head. The text notes that this is only one narration, implying there is another opinion.

Key Terms
غسل الميت (ghusl al-mayyit): The ritual washing of a deceased Muslim before burial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a difference between washing the deceased and the living?
A) Starting with the face vs. hands
B) Omitting mouth and nose rinse
C) Washing the feet three times
D) Not wiping the head in wudu

Key Takeaway
Four specific differences govern ghusl al-mayyit: start with face, no mouth/nose rinse, wash feet immediately, and omit head wiping (according to the preferred narration).

Distinguishing Zakat from Sadaqat al-Fitr: Four Key Furuat
Listing four differences between zakat and sadaqat al-fitr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four key differences between zakat and sadaqat al-fitr and apply each difference to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Zakat and sadaqat al-fitr are distinct obligations with different conditions, recipients, timing, and permissibility of early payment.

A mufti must distinguish between zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, as they share the name 'obligatory charity' but have very different rulings. This lesson presents four furuat that clarify the distinction, forming a useful memorization anchor for the practicing scholar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first difference: nisab growth requirement
The nisab of zakat requires the property to have growth potential (nama'), even if only by estimation. Sadaqat al-fitr has no such requirement; its nisab is food grains.
This is a condition for zakat to be obligatory.
2
Identify the second difference: permissibility of giving to a dhimmi
Zakat cannot be given to a non-Muslim dhimmi, whereas sadaqat al-fitr may be given to them according to the dominant Hanafi view.
Recipient eligibility differs.
3
Identify the third difference: time limit
Zakat has no fixed time for payment; one may delay without sin (though it should be paid eventually). Sadaqat al-fitr has a specific time: from the beginning of Shawwal before the Eid prayer; delaying past the first day is sinful.
Time constraint only applies to sadaqat al-fitr.
4
Identify the fourth difference: early payment before possession of nisab
Zakat may not be given before one possesses the nisab for a full lunar year. Sadaqat al-fitr can be given after the 'existence of the head' (i.e., after the start of Ramadan when the obligation is established).
The phrase 'wujud al-ra's' may refer to the appearance of the crescent of Shawwal.
5
Understand the underlying qawida
The differences can be summarized as a qawida: 'Al-zakatu wa sadaqatu al-fitr yatafarraqani fi arba'ati umur: nisab, mustahiqq, waqt, wa taqdim.'
This aids memorization and fatwa application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الزكاة وصدقة الفطر:
يشترط في نصاب الزكاة النمو ولو تقديرًا بخلاف نصابها، ولا يجوز دفعها لذمي بخلافها، ولا وقت لها ولصدقة الفطر وقت محدود يأثم بالتأخير عن اليوم الأول، ولا يجوز تعجيلها قبل ملك النصاب بخلافها بعد وجود الرأس.
What distinguishes zakat from sadaqat al-fitr: In the nisab of zakat, growth (nama') is required, even if by estimation, unlike its nisab (of sadaqat al-fitr). And it is not permissible to give it (zakat) to a dhimmi, unlike it (sadaqat al-fitr). And it (zakat) has no time limit, while sadaqat al-fitr has a limited time; one sins by delaying past the first day. And it (zakat) may not be given before possessing the nisab, unlike it (sadaqat al-fitr) after the existence of the head (i.e., after the month of Ramadan begins).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage outlines four differences between zakat (obligatory alms on wealth) and sadaqat al-fitr (obligatory alms at the end of Ramadan). First, the nisab (minimum threshold) for zakat requires the property to have growth potential (nama'), such as livestock, gold, silver, or trade goods, even if this growth is only potential (taqdiran); the nisab for sadaqat al-fitr is food grains and does not require growth. Second, zakat cannot be given to a non-Muslim dhimmi (protected citizen), while sadaqat al-fitr may be given to them according to some opinions. Third, zakat has no fixed time; it is obligatory from the moment nisab is held for a lunar year, but there is no sin for delaying payment as long as it is paid eventually, whereas sadaqat al-fitr has a specific time (from the beginning of Shawwal before the Eid prayer) and delaying past the first day of Eid is sinful. Fourth, zakat cannot be given before one possesses the nisab for a full year, while sadaqat al-fitr can be given after the 'existence of the head' (i.e., after the start of Ramadan when the obligation is confirmed). The phrase 'ba'da wujud al-ra's' may mean after the appearance of the head of the month (i.e., after seeing the crescent of Shawwal) or after the obligation is established.

Key Terms
الزكاة (al-zakat): Obligatory charity on specific types of wealth held for a lunar year, one of the five pillars.
صدقة الفطر (sadaqat al-fitr): Obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan by every free Muslim who possesses excess provisions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about giving charity to a non-Muslim dhimmi?
A) Both zakat and sadaqat al-fitr can be given to a dhimmi.
B) Zakat cannot be given to a dhimmi, but sadaqat al-fitr may be.
C) Only zakat can be given to a dhimmi.
D) Neither can be given to a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
Zakat and sadaqat al-fitr differ in nisab condition, recipient permissibility, time limit, and permissibility of early payment.

Differences between Tamattu' and Qiran in Hajj
Listing differences between tamattu' and qiran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two key differences between tamattu' and qiran, and apply them to distinguish between the two forms of Hajj in a given scenario.

Core Idea
In tamattu', you break ihram after Umrah; in qiran, you stay in ihram from miqat to the end.

This passage from the Ashbah wa al-Nazhair lists the core distinctions between tamattu' and qiran—two of the three types of Hajj. Understanding these differences is essential for correctly guiding pilgrims in their rites. The focus is on two points: the timing of exiting ihram (tahallul) and the location of ihram for each rite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two differences
Read the Arabic text carefully. The passage mentions two contrasts: (1) tahallul after Umrah depends on bringing a sacrificial animal, and (2) the ihram for Umrah vs. combined ihram. List them.
Use both the Arabic and translation.
2
Apply to a pilgrim scenario
Imagine a pilgrim who arrives at the miqat intending tamattu'. He performs Umrah, then exits ihram. Later, he enters ihram for Hajj inside Mecca. Does this match the description? Yes, that is tamattu'. If he had intended both from the miqat and stayed in ihram, it would be qiran.
Practice distinguishing based on the two criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه التمتع والقران:
يتحلَّل من العمرة بعد الفراغ منها إن لم يسق الهدي بخلافه، يحرم بالعمرة وحدها من الميقات ويأتي بأفعالها ثم يحرم بالحج من الحرم بخلاف القارن فإنه يحرم بهما معا من الميقات.
What distinguishes tamattu' from qiran: He becomes free from ihram for Umrah after completing it if he has not brought the sacrificial animal, unlike it (qiran). He assumes ihram for Umrah alone from the miqat and performs its actions, then assumes ihram for Hajj from the Haram, unlike the qarin, for he assumes ihram for both together from the miqat.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage highlights two main differences between tamattu' (performing Umrah and Hajj separately with a break in ihram) and qiran (combining Umrah and Hajj with a single ihram). In tamattu', after completing the Umrah, the pilgrim can exit ihram (tahallul) if he did not bring a sacrificial animal (hady). In qiran, the pilgrim remains in ihram until Hajj. In tamattu', the pilgrim enters ihram for Umrah alone at the miqat (appointed station), performs the Umrah, then makes a new ihram for Hajj from the Haram (e.g., Mecca). In qiran, the pilgrim enters ihram for both Umrah and Hajj simultaneously from the miqat.

Key Terms
التمتع (al-tamattu'): A type of Hajj where one performs Umrah first, then Hajj in the same year, with a break in ihram.
القران (al-qiran): A type of Hajj where one combines Umrah and Hajj with a single ihram from the miqat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In tamattu', where does the pilgrim assume ihram for Hajj?
A) From the miqat for Umrah
B) From the Haram (e.g., Mecca)
C) From Arafat
D) From Muzdalifah

Key Takeaway
The two key differences between tamattu' and qiran are: (1) tahallul after Umrah (allowed in tamattu' unless hady is brought) vs. no tahallul in qiran; (2) place of ihram for Hajj (from the Haram in tamattu', from the miqat in qiran).

Differences Between Gift (Hibah) and Discharge (Ibra')
Listing two differences between hibah and ibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two key differences between hibah and ibra' under Hanafi law and apply them to determine the rulings of acceptance and revocability in practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Hibah requires acceptance and is revocable; ibra' needs no acceptance and is irrevocable.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, gifts (hibah) and legal discharges (ibra') may seem similar, but they differ in two fundamental ways. This lesson presents those differences clearly, based on the legal maxims from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir.

Guided Path
1
Identify each legal act
Hibah is a gift—transfer of ownership without compensation. Ibra' is a discharge—waiving a debt or right unilaterally.
2
Compare the requirement of acceptance
Hibah requires acceptance (qabul) by the donee to be valid, while ibra' does not require acceptance by the debtor.
Acceptance is a condition (shart) for hibah but not for ibra'.
3
Compare revocability
The giver in hibah may revoke (ruju') the gift if no legal impediment exists (e.g., gift to a relative or consumed property), whereas ibra' is absolutely irrevocable once made.
Revocability is a property of hibah but not of ibra'.
4
Apply the differences
When giving a gift, ensure acceptance and consider possible revocation. When discharging a debt, no acceptance is needed and it cannot be undone.
This affects legal practice and fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الهبة والإبراء:
يشترط لها القبول بخلافه، وله الرجوع فيها عند عدم المانع بخلافه مطلقا.
What distinguishes hibah from ibra': Acceptance is a condition for it (hibah), unlike it (ibra'). And the giver has the right to revoke it (hibah) when there is no preventing factor, unlike it (ibra'), absolutely.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents two differences between hibah (gift) and ibra' (legal discharge or waiver of a debt). First, hibah requires acceptance (qabul) by the donee to be valid, while ibra' is a unilateral act that does not require the debtor's acceptance. Second, the giver in hibah may revoke the gift (ruju') if there is no legal impediment (such as the gift being to a relative, or the subject being consumed), whereas ibra' is irrevocable (la ruju'], absolutely, once it is made.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; voluntary transfer of ownership of property without compensation.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Legal discharge; waiving a debt or right unilaterally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a person revoke an ibra' (discharge of debt) according to this passage?
A) Yes, under all conditions
B) Yes, if no preventing factor
C) No, absolutely not
D) Only with the debtor's consent

Key Takeaway
Hibah requires acceptance and is potentially revocable; ibra' is effective without acceptance and irrevocable.

Six Differences Between Ijarah and Sale
Listing differences between lease (ijarah) and sale (bay')

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six differences between ijarah and sale and apply each difference to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Ijarah and sale differ fundamentally in time, ownership, and grounds for rescission.

This passage lists six key differences between ijarah (lease) and bay' (sale). These are practical furuat that a mufti must know to correctly classify contracts and resolve disputes. Understanding each point will help you distinguish when a contract is valid or dissolved in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify each difference
Read the Arabic text line by line. The passage lists six distinct rulings where ijarah and sale diverge. Make sure you can articulate each one clearly.
The first difference is about time stipulation (ta'qit).
2
Classify the ruling for each
For each difference, determine whether the rule applies to validity (sihha), ownership (milk), or dissolution (faskh). This helps organize the differences into categories.
3
Link to broader principles
Consider how each difference stems from the fundamental nature of ijarah as a sale of usufruct (manafi') and bay' as a sale of tangible property ('ayn). This maximizes retention.
Time-bound contracts fit usufruct; immediate ownership fits tangible goods.
4
Formulate a legal maxim
Try to express each difference as a qawida (maxim) or a sub-rule. For example: 'Al-ta'qit yufsid al-bay' wa yusihh al-ijarah.' This transforms the list into reusable principles.
The first difference can be a dabt: 'تأقيت البيع يفسده، وتأقيت الإجارة يصححها'.
5
Apply to new cases
Test your understanding by inventing a short scenario for each difference. For example: what if a lease is silent on duration? (It would be fasid, but sale without duration is mubah? Actually sale without time is valid but may be deferred.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الإجارة والبيع:
التأقيت يفسده ويصححها ويملك العوض فيه بالعقد وفيها لا. إلا بواحد من أربعة وتفسخ بالأعذار بخلافه، وتنفسخ بعيب حادث، بخلافه، وتنفسخ بموت أحدهما إذا عقدها لنفسه بخلافه، وإذا هلك الثمن قبل قبضه لا يبطل البيع وإذا هلكت الأجرة العين قبله انفسخت.
What distinguishes ijarah from sale: Specifying a time invalidates it (sale) and validates it (ijarah). The consideration is owned by the contract in sale, but not in ijarah except by one of four (ways). And it (ijarah) is dissolved by excuses, unlike it (sale). And it is dissolved by a supervening defect, unlike it. And it is dissolved by the death of one of the parties if the contract was for his own benefit, unlike it. And if the price is destroyed before receipt, the sale is not voided; but if the hired object (or wage?) is destroyed before its receipt, the contract is dissolved.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage lists several differences between ijarah (lease or hire) and bay' (sale). The first: stipulating a time period (ta'qit) invalidates a sale (since sale requires immediate delivery) but validates an ijarah (since leases are by nature time-bound). Second: in a sale, ownership of the consideration (thaman) transfers immediately upon contract; in ijarah, ownership of the rental fee (ujrah) does not transfer immediately except in four specific ways (e.g., when the lessee takes possession of the leased asset). Third: ijarah can be terminated due to valid excuses (a'dhar) such as illness or travel, whereas a sale is not rescinded for excuses. Fourth: ijarah is dissolved by a defect that arises after the contract (hadith), but a sale is not dissolved for a later defect (unless a defect was present at the time). Fifth: ijarah terminates with the death of either party if the contract was made for the benefit of the deceased party (i.e., personal service), but sale is not voided by death (the heirs inherit the rights). Sixth: if the price (thaman) is destroyed before delivery, the sale is not automatically void (the buyer is still liable), but if the hired asset (al-ajr al-'ayn) is destroyed before delivery, the ijarah is dissolved. Note: 'al-ajr' may refer to the hired object or the wage; context suggests the hired object.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract of lease or hire for the use of an asset or services in exchange for payment.
البيع (al-bay'): Sale; exchange of property for consideration.
التأقيت (al-ta'qit): Stipulation of a time period in a contract.
العوض (al-'iwad): The consideration (price or rental fee) in a contract.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Dissolution or rescission of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a lease (ijarah) if the hired object is destroyed before the lessee takes possession?
A) The contract continues and the lessee must pay
B) The contract is dissolved
C) The lessee gets a replacement
D) The contract becomes a sale

Key Takeaway
Ijarah and sale differ in six key rules: time, ownership, excuses, defects, death, and destruction before delivery—each rooted in the nature of the subject matter (usufruct vs. property).

Differences Between Wife and Slave Woman: A Hanafi Legal Maxim with Branches
Listing four furuat under the maxim differentiating wife and slave woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four legal differences between a free wife and a female slave, and apply the implicit maxim to derive rulings for new analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
A free wife has rights of qism, mahr, determined nafaqah, and is limited to four; a slave woman lacks these.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir lists four key furuat (branches) that distinguish a free wife from a female slave in Hanafi law. Understanding these differences is essential for a mufti when answering questions about marriages involving slaves or when dealing with contemporary analogous issues. The passage itself serves as a minor maxim (dabt) that governs the application of marital rulings based on personal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal persons
The passage contrasts al-zawjah (free wife) and al-amah (female slave). Note that the slave woman is not a wife of her owner but can be married to another; her legal capacity differs.
2
List the four differences
From the text: 1) No qism (division of nights) for the slave woman. 2) No limit on the number of slave women one may own, unlike wives (max four). 3) Her maintenance is not fixed and does not drop due to nushuz, unlike the wife. 4) No mahr for her, unlike the wife.
Memorize these four points.
3
Understand the basis for each difference
For each difference, the underlying reason is the slave's status as property. Qism is a right of a free wife; a slave woman has no right to it. The limit of four wives is for free women; slaves are property and can be unlimited. Maintenance of a wife is a contractual right; for a slave, it is part of ownership and not lost by disobedience. Mahr is the exclusive right of a free wife; a slave's mahr goes to her owner.
4
Extract the implicit qawida
The general principle is: 'A free wife possesses certain marital rights that a slave woman does not, due to her status as a free person versus property.' This dabt (sub-maxim) restricts the broader maxim 'al-mar'ah al-hurrah wa al-amah mukhtalifat al-ahkam.'
This qawida is used to resolve furuat in nikah and related chapters.
5
Apply to new furuat
Extend the reasoning to other rights: e.g., a slave woman cannot be a witness in marriage, cannot have custody of children if the mother is free, etc. Use the status-based distinction as the illa.
Practice with hypotheticals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الزوجة والأمة:
لا قسم للأمة بخلافها، ولا حصر لعدد الإماء بخلاف الزوجات، ولا تقدر نفقتها بخلاف الزوجة فإنها بحسب حالهما ولا يسقطها النشوز بخلاف الزوجة، ولا صداق لها بخلاف الزوجة.
What distinguishes the wife from the slave woman: There is no division of nights for the slave woman, unlike her (the wife). There is no limit to the number of slave women, unlike wives. Her maintenance is not determined, unlike the wife, for it (wife's maintenance) is according to their condition; and it (maintenance) does not drop due to nushuz (disobedience), unlike the wife. And there is no mahr for her, unlike the wife.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage lists four legal differences between a wife (zawjah) and a female slave (amah) with respect to marital-like rights. First, a wife has the right to qism (equal division of nights among co-wives), while a slave woman does not. Second, there is no limit on the number of slave women one may own, but a free man is limited to four wives. Third, the maintenance (nafaqah) of a wife is determined based on the husband's and wife's circumstances, whereas the maintenance of a slave woman is not fixed in the same way. Moreover, a wife loses her right to maintenance if she is nashiz (disobedient), but a slave woman's maintenance is not dropped for nushuz (since she is owned). Fourth, a wife receives mahr (dower), while a slave woman does not because she is not married to her owner; if a slave woman is married to someone else, her mahr goes to her owner.

Key Terms
الزوجة (al-zawjah): A free wife in a valid marriage contract.
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave owned by a master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a right of a wife but not of a female slave?
A) Maintenance
B) Mahr (dower)
C) Clothing
D) Shelter

Key Takeaway
A free wife has distinct legal rights that a slave woman lacks, forming a key maxim in Hanafi family jurisprudence.

Three Differences Between Nafaqah of Wife and Relative
Listing three legal differences between two types of maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three differences between nafaqah of a wife and nafaqah of a relative, and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A wife's maintenance is a fixed debt; a relative's is conditional and lapses with time.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir distills the core distinctions between two overlapping obligations: the husband's duty to maintain his wife, and the obligation to support needy relatives. These differences matter enormously in practice: one is a fixed debt that never disappears, the other is a flexible duty that can vanish. By memorizing these three points, you can resolve common questions that reach a mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three differences
Read the passage carefully. It lists three points of contrast: (1) How the amount is determined (muqaddarah vs kifayah), (2) Whether the obligation lapses with time after non-payment, and (3) What conditions trigger the obligation (poverty of recipient, wealth of provider).
The Arabic uses 'Nafaqatuha' (her maintenance) and 'Nafaqatuhu' (his maintenance of the relative).
2
Explain the reasoning behind each difference
For each difference, ask: Why does the rule differ? The wife's maintenance is a contractual right arising from marriage, so it becomes a debt. The relative's maintenance is a charitable duty, so it is contingent on need and ability, and lapses if the need is not pressed.
3
Apply each difference to a hypothetical case
Take a scenario: A husband delays paying his wife's nafaqah for a year; does he still owe it? Yes. A brother delays paying his needy sister's nafaqah for a year; does he still owe it? No, if she managed without it. Another scenario: the wife is wealthy; does she still get nafaqah? Yes (though she might not be entitled if she is nashiz?). Actually, wife's nafaqah is not conditioned on her poverty. The relative must be poor.
Refer back to the text for exact conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه نفقة الزوجة والقريب:
نفقتها مقدرة بحالهما ونفقته بالكفاية، ونفقتها لا تسقط بمضي الزمان بعد التقدير أو الاصطلاح بخلاف نفقته وشرط نفقته إعساره وزمانته ويسار المنفق بخلاف نفقتها.
What distinguishes the maintenance of the wife from that of a relative: Her maintenance is estimated according to both (husband and wife's conditions), while his maintenance (of the relative) is according to sufficiency (kifayah). Her maintenance does not drop with the passage of time after estimation or agreement, unlike his maintenance. And the condition for his maintenance is the relative's poverty and chronic illness and the ease of the provider, unlike her maintenance.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage outlines differences between nafaqah (maintenance) of a wife and nafaqah of a needy relative (qarib). First, a wife's maintenance is fixed (muqaddarah) based on both spouses' financial and social conditions, while a relative's maintenance is determined by the principle of sufficiency (kifayah), i.e., providing what is needed for survival. Second, a wife's maintenance does not lapse by the passage of time once it is estimated or agreed upon; if the husband delays payment, it remains a debt. However, a relative's maintenance may lapse with time if the provider did not pay it and the relative managed without it, because it is not a fixed debt. Third, the maintenance of a relative is conditional upon the relative being poor (i'asar) and chronically ill (zamanah) and the provider being wealthy (yasar al-munfiq), whereas a wife's maintenance is obligatory regardless of her wealth (though she is not entitled if she is wealthy and refuses) and does not require the husband's ease beyond his ability.

Key Terms
نفقة الزوجة (nafaqat al-zawjah): Maintenance provided by the husband to the wife, including food, clothing, housing, etc.
نفقة القريب (nafaqat al-qarib): Maintenance of needy relatives, such as parents, children, etc.
مقدرة (muqaddarah): Fixed or estimated according to a standard (here, the condition of both spouses).
كفاية (kifayah): Sufficiency; enough to meet basic needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a relative's maintenance if it is not paid over time, according to this passage?
A) It remains a debt on the provider
B) It lapses (falls off)
C) It is doubled
D) It becomes a charitable donation

Key Takeaway
A wife's maintenance is a fixed debt that does not lapse; a relative's maintenance is a conditional duty that lapses if not claimed promptly.

Differences in Rulings: Apostate vs. Original Disbeliever
Listing differences in rulings between an apostate and an original disbeliever

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the eleven specific rulings that distinguish an apostate from an original disbeliever in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Apostasy incurs harsher penalties than original disbelief: no jizya, marriage invalid, property frozen.

This lesson examines the canonical list from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir that contrasts the legal treatment of an apostate (murtadd) with an original disbeliever (kafir asli). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas on apostasy-related cases. The rulings range from capital punishment to inheritance and burial, each underscored by the principle that apostasy is a rebellion against the faith, not mere difference of belief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Eleven Differences
Read the Arabic passage and list each ruling that applies specifically to the apostate. Note the key verb or phrase for each: لا يقر, لا يصح نكاحه, لا تحل ذبيحته, etc.
Memorize the list in order; it is a standard mnemonic in Hanafi manuals.
2
Contrast with the Original Disbeliever
For each apostate ruling, recall the corresponding ruling for an original disbeliever. For example, an original disbeliever may be allowed to remain as a dhimmi with jizya, an apostate may not.
Use the key term definitions as a reference.
3
Extract the Governing Legal Maxim
The passage itself is a collection of furu' (branches) under a general maxim: the rulings on apostates are more severe. Formulate the maxim in Arabic, e.g., 'المرتد يخالف الكافر الأصلي في أحكامه'.
This maxim is a minor (sughra) qawida that restricts the general rule that non-Muslims are treated alike.
4
Analyze the Rationale Behind Each Difference
Reflect on why apostasy carries harsher penalties. For instance, the apostate's wealth is suspended because his status is ambiguous—he may repent. His marriage is dissolved because a Muslim woman cannot remain married to a non-Muslim man.
This step deepens understanding beyond memorization.
5
Apply to a Hypothetical Scenario
Given a case where a Muslim man renounces Islam, determine which of these eleven rulings apply and the immediate legal consequences.
Practice applying the whole list to a concrete fact pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه المرتد والكافر الأصلي:
لا يقرّ المرتد ولو بجزية ولا يصح نكاحه ولا تحل ذبيحته، ويهدر دمه، ويوقف ماله، وتصرفاته، ولا يُسبَى ولا يُفادَى ولا يمنّ عليه ولا يرث ولا يورث، ولا يدفن في مقابر أهل ملة، ولا يتبعه ولده فيها.
What distinguishes the apostate from the original disbeliever: The apostate is not allowed to remain (under protection) even with jizya. His marriage is not valid. His slaughtered meat is not permissible. His blood is deemed forfeit. His wealth and transactions are suspended. He is not taken captive, nor ransomed, nor given favor. He does not inherit nor is he inherited. He is not buried in the graveyards of the people of a religion. His children do not follow him in that (i.e., in his apostasy).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage lists several legal consequences that apply specifically to an apostate (murtadd) as opposed to an original disbeliever (kafir asli). The apostate is not granted aman (protection) even if he agrees to pay jizya; he must be executed or repent. His existing marriage is dissolved. Any meat from animals he slaughters is not halal. His blood is unprotected (mubah al-dam). His property and all transactions are frozen pending his return to Islam or execution. He is not to be enslaved, ransomed, or released as a favor (as may be done with captured enemies). He neither inherits from others nor do his relatives inherit from him. He cannot be buried in Muslim or any other religious community's graveyard. And his children are not considered followers of his apostasy; they remain Muslim if they were born before his apostasy. These severe penalties apply because apostasy is considered a rebellion against the faith, whereas an original disbeliever may be allowed to remain in dar al-harb or live as a dhimmi under certain conditions.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A person who was Muslim and then renounces Islam.
الكافر الأصلي (al-kafir al-asli): A person who was born to non-Muslim parents and never accepted Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the wealth of an apostate according to this passage?
A) It is distributed to his heirs
B) It is confiscated by the state
C) It is suspended (waqafa)
D) He retains it if he pays jizya

Key Takeaway
The apostate is subject to eleven distinct, harsher rulings compared to an original disbeliever, reflecting apostasy as a rebellion against the faith rather than a mere difference in belief.

Differences Between Wife and Slave Girl
Listing of four furu' differences between wife and slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four key legal differences between a wife and a slave girl in Hanafi fiqh and apply the underlying qawa'id to new cases.

Core Idea
A wife is a contractual partner with rights; a slave girl is property without those rights.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir concisely enumerates four foundational distinctions between a wife (zawjah) and a female slave (ummah) in Hanafi law. These differences are not arbitrary; they stem from the core legal nature of marriage (nikah) as a contract conferring mutual obligations, versus ownership (milk al-yamin) which is unilateral. Understanding these distinctions sharpens your ability to apply qawa'id usuliyyah in ifta' and to reason from fundamental principles to specific furu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first difference: Qasam (equal division of nights)
A wife is entitled to equal division of nights among co-wives; a slave girl has no such right. This is because qasam is a wajib right for wives, not for mere property.
Qasam is wajib upon the husband if he has multiple wives, but not for slave girls.
2
Identify the second difference: Limit on number
A man may marry up to four wives, but there is no limit on the number of slave girls he can own. This is based on the Quranic permission: 'Marry what seems good to you of women, two, three, or four' (4:3).
Ownership (milk al-yamin) is not restricted by number, but marriage is.
3
Identify the third difference: Maintenance (nafaqah) and nushuz
A wife's maintenance is determined by the circumstances of both spouses and it is fixed; a slave girl's maintenance is merely sufficient for her needs and is not fixed. Additionally, if a wife becomes nushuz (disobedient), she loses her right to maintenance, but a slave girl's maintenance continues regardless.
Nafaqah for the wife is a wajib right subject to ta'yeen; for a slave girl it is the owner's obligation without equivalent consequences.
4
Identify the fourth difference: Dower (mahr)
A wife is entitled to mahr (dower) upon marriage; a slave girl has no dower. Mahr is a direct consequence of the marriage contract, while ownership does not entail any such payment.
Mahr is an essential element (rukn) of the marriage contract; slave girls are not parties to a contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه الزوجة والأمة : لا قسم للأمة بخلافها، ولا حصر لعدد الإماء بخلاف الزوجات، ولا تقدر نفقتها بخلاف الزوجة فإنها بحسب حالهما ولا يسقطها النشوز بخلاف الزوجة، ولا صداق لها بخلاف الزوجة .
What distinguishes the wife from the slave girl: There is no turn [in cohabitation] for a slave girl, unlike the wife. There is no limit on the number of slave girls, unlike wives. Her maintenance is not fixed, unlike the wife's, for it depends on their circumstances, and it is not dropped by nushuz, unlike the wife. She has no dower, unlike the wife.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage outlines four differences. First, a wife is entitled to equal division of nights (qasam) among co-wives; a slave girl has no such right. Second, a man may marry up to four wives, but there is no limit on the number of slave girls he can possess. Third, a wife's maintenance is determined by her husband's and her own circumstances (wealth, status) and it is fixed; a slave girl's maintenance is not fixed and is merely sufficient for her needs. Also, if a wife becomes disobedient (nushuz), she loses her right to maintenance, but a slave girl's maintenance continues regardless. Fourth, a wife is entitled to a dower (mahr), while a slave girl has no dower.

Key Terms
الزوجة (al-zawjah): Lawful wife through marriage contract
الأمة (al-ammah): Female slave owned by master
القسم (al-qasm): Equal division of nights among co-wives
النشوز (al-nushuz): Disobedience of wife to husband
المهر (al-mahr): Dower paid to wife upon marriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a right of a wife that a slave girl does NOT have?
A) Right to maintenance.
B) Right to equal division of nights (qasam).
C) Right to be free from nushuz consequences.
D) Right to unlimited number in the household.

Key Takeaway
The four differences between a wife and a slave girl—qasam, numerical limit, nafaqah (including nushuz effect), and mahr—stem from the fundamental legal distinction between marriage (contract) and ownership (property).

Eight Distinctions Between Apostate and Original Non-Muslim
Differences in legal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight key legal differences between an apostate and an original non-Muslim according to the Hanafi school, and apply these distinctions in fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
Apostasy severs all legal protections: marriage, property, life, and inheritance rules differ fundamentally from an original non-Muslim.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents a terse enumeration of eight points where the apostate (murtadd) is treated distinctly from the born non-Muslim (kafir asli). Understanding these differences is crucial for issuing fatwas on conversion, inheritance, and penalties. The contrasts underscore that apostasy is considered a capital offense and a breach of the social contract, unlike the protected status of a dhimmi.

Guided Path
1
List Each Point
Read the passage and list the eight distinct rulings mentioned for the apostate. Write them down in order.
The passage uses 'لا' to negate each right or consequence.
2
Identify the Ruling for the Apostate
For each point, state the explicit legal consequence for the apostate (e.g., his marriage is invalid, his slaughter is not permissible).
Focus on the murtadd only.
3
Contrast with Original Non-Muslim
For each point, recall or research the parallel ruling for the original non-Muslim (dhimmi or harbi). Identify where the two statuses differ.
E.g., original kafir may be allowed to remain with jizya; apostate is not.
4
Analyze the Rationale
Reflect on the reasoning behind each difference. Consider the gravity of apostasy as renouncing faith after acceptance.
Apostasy is seen as treason against the Ummah, hence harsher.
5
Synthesize the Principle
Formulate a general maxim: the apostate loses all protections that a dhimmi enjoys, and his life, property, and family ties are suspended until repentance or execution.
This principle guides fatwa on conversion cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما افترق فيه المرتد والكافر الأصلي : لا يقرّ المرتد ولو بجزية ولا يصح نكاحه ولا تحل ذبيحته، ويهدر دمه، ويوقف ماله وتصرفاته، ولا يُسبَى ولا يُفادَى ولا يمنّ عليه ولا يرث ولا يورث، ولا يدفن في مقابر أهل ملة، ولا يتبعه ولده فيها.
What distinguishes the apostate from the original non-Muslim: The apostate is not allowed to remain [in Islam] even with jizya. His marriage is not valid, his slaughter is not permissible, his blood is licit, his property and transactions are suspended. He is not taken captive, not ransomed, not granted amnesty. He does not inherit and is not inherited from. He is not buried in the cemeteries of any religious community, nor do his children follow him in that [religion].
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage enumerates the severe legal consequences of apostasy in Hanafi fiqh, contrasting with the original kafir. The apostate is not given the option to stay with jizya; he must be killed or repent. His marriage to a Muslim woman becomes invalid, his slaughter (dhabihah) is not halal, and his blood may be shed (i.e., execution is prescribed). His property is suspended until his repentance or death. He cannot be taken as a war captive (because he is treated as a murtadd, not a harbi), nor redeemed or given amnesty. He does not inherit from Muslim relatives, nor do Muslims inherit from him. He cannot be buried in Muslim cemeteries, nor in Jewish or Christian cemeteries, and his children are not considered to follow his religion (they remain Muslim).

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): One who leaves Islam after being Muslim
الكافر الأصلي (al-kafir al-asli): Someone born into or originally a non-Muslim religion
الجزية (al-jizyah): Tax imposed on non-Muslims for protection
يهدر دمه (yuhdar damuhu): His blood is licit; i.e., killing him without retaliation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following applies to an apostate but not to an original non-Muslim?
A) His marriage is valid if contracted while Muslim.
B) He can be ransomed if captured.
C) His blood is licit (execution prescribed).
D) He can inherit from Muslim relatives.

Key Takeaway
An apostate is treated as a non-Muslim with added penalties due to the gravity of leaving Islam, affecting all legal capacities from marriage to inheritance.
